Received 
      20th March 2021
    First published on 24th September 2021AbstractSince the discovery of N-doped carbon nanotubes as the first carbon-based metal-free electrocatalyst (C-MFEC) for oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in 2009, C-MFECs have shown multifunctional electrocatalytic activities for many reactions beyond ORR, such as oxygen evolution reaction (OER), hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), carbon dioxide reduction reaction (CO2RR), nitrogen reduction reaction (NRR), and hydrogen peroxide production reaction (H2O2PR). Consequently, C-MFECs have attracted a great deal of interest for various applications, including metal–air batteries, water splitting devices, regenerative fuel cells, solar cells, fuel and chemical production, water purification, to mention a few. By altering the electronic configuration and/or modulating their spin angular momentum, both heteroatom(s) doping and structural defects (e.g., atomic vacancy, edge) have been demonstrated to create catalytic active sites in the skeleton of graphitic carbon materials. Although certain C-MFECs have been made to be comparable to or even better than their counterparts based on noble metals, transition metals and/or their hybrids, further research and development are necessary in order to translate C-MFECs for practical applications. In this article, we present a timely and comprehensive, but critical, review on recent advancements in the field of C-MFECs within the past five years or so by discussing various types of electrocatalytic reactions catalyzed by C-MFECs. An emphasis is given to potential applications of C-MFECs for energy conversion and storage. The structure–property relationship for and mechanistic understanding of C-MFECs will also be discussed, along with the current challenges and future perspectives.
Chuangang Hu
Chuangang Hu is a professor at the College of Chemical Engineering, Beijing University of Chemical Technology. He received his MS from Henan Normal University in 2011, and his PhD from Beijing Institute of Technology in 2015. After receiving his PhD degree, he continued his postdoctoral studies for 5 years at Case Western Reserve University (4 years), USA and the University of New South Wales (1 year), Australia. His research interests focus on the design and preparation of advanced carbon-based materials for environmental remediation, energy conversion, and storage.
Rajib Paul
Dr Rajib Paul studied BSc and MSc in Physics and PhD in Materials Science and Nanotechnology from Jadavpur University in 2010. He joined Case Western Reserve University in 2016 as a senior research associate after his postdoctoral studies at Birck Nanotechnology Center in Purdue University. He works on innovative materials development and their atomic scale manipulation for efficient thermal and electrochemical energy storage and conversion. He is recently also involved with polymer processing and understanding the ageing mechanism in polymers and energy systems to ensure public safety.
Quanbin Dai
Quanbin Dai received his BS degree in Biology in 2013 and his PhD degree in Macromolecular Science & Engineering in 2021 from Case Western Reserve University. His research interests focus on carbon-based materials for biomedical and energy related applications.
Liming Dai
Liming Dai is an Australian Laureate Fellow, a Scientia & SHARP Professor and the Director of Australian Carbon Materials Centre at University of New South Wales Sydney, Australia. Before joining UNSW, he spent 10 years with CSIRO (1992–2002) and was an associate professor of polymer engineering at the University of Akron (2002–2004), the Wright Brothers Institute Endowed Chair Professor of Nanomaterials at the University of Dayton (2004–2009), and the Kent Hale Smith Professor in the Department of Macromolecular Science and Engineering at Case Western Reserve University (2009–2019). He is a Fellow of the National Academy of Inventors (USA), Fellow of the American Institute for Medical and Biological Engineering, Fellow of the Royal Society of Chemistry, Fellow of the European Academy of Sciences, and Fellow of the International Association of Advanced Materials. His expertise covers the synthesis, functionalization, and device fabrication of conjugated polymers and carbon nanomaterials for energy-related and biomedical applications.
1. Introduction
The continued expansion of electric automobile, electronic, and communication industries1–3 has led to an ever-increasing demand for compact and portable electronic devices, which requires advanced energy materials for cost-effective and efficient energy conversion and storage with no harmful effect on the environment.4–6 In this regard, some renewable energy technologies, including fuel cells, water splitting electrolyzers, and metal–air batteries, have been demonstrated to perform energy conversion and storage with minimum or no environmental concern. On the other hand, certain innovative electrocatalytic technologies have been developed to cost-effectively produce value-added chemicals and/or energy materials, such as ammonia (NH3), hydrogen peroxide (H2O2), and many other hydrocarbons (e.g., CH3OH, C2H2).1,7
As a heart of the aforementioned technologies, several seemingly simple, but important, electrochemical reactions, such as oxygen reduction reaction (ORR), oxygen evolution reaction (OER), hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), carbon dioxide reduction, and evolution reactions (CO2RR and CO2ER, respectively), nitrogen reduction reaction (NRR), and hydrogen peroxide production reaction (H2O2PR), need to be addressed.1,8 Traditionally, noble metal-based electrodes, such as platinum (Pt), palladium (Pd), iridium (Ir), ruthenium (Ru), and their various compounds, are well-known as the best catalysts for such electrochemical reactions.4,9 However, the high cost and scarcity of these noble metals have hindered their large-scale applications. Therefore, their alloys and compounds with transition metals, and even transition metals only, have been investigated extensively as cheaper alternatives of noble-metal-based catalysts for electrocatalysis.1,5 However, these transition metal-based electrocatalysts are still too expensive for large-scale applications and have multiple disadvantages, including a low selectivity, susceptibility to time-dependent drift, poor durability in harsh electrolytes (particularly acidic), easy to be poisoned by by-product gases, and negative environmental effects.4,5 In 2009, N-doped carbon nanotubes were discovered as an effective carbon-based metal-free electrocatalyst (C-MFEC) for ORR in fuel cells6 – one of the most promising renewable energy technologies to transfer hydrogen into clean electricity [http://americanhistory.si.edu/fuelcells/alk/alk3.htm]. This pioneering work subverts the traditional belief that noble-metal-based catalysts (e.g., Pt) are necessary for the ORR in fuel cells. Since then, C-MFEC has emerged as a new field of research worldwide (Fig. 1).4,8,10
 Fig. 1  Electrocatalytic activities of C-MFECs. 
The doping-induced charge transfer from carbon atoms to the adjacent nitrogen atoms caused change in the chemisorption mode of O2 to facilitate the ORR on the N-doped C-MFECs.6 Detection of this innovative. This new fundamental understanding of ORR mechanism in nitrogen-doped C-MFECs is important, which has been applied to the development of various metal-free efficient ORR catalysts for other reactions even beyond ORR.4,8,10 Over the last decade or so, the field of C-MFECs has grown enormously.8 As a result, heteroatom doping, defect doping, and/or structural modification have been used to develop various multifunctional C-MFECs for ORR/OER/HER,11–13 two-electron transfer ORR to produce H2O2 (an energy carrier and green oxidizer),14 reduction of triiodide to iodide (I2RR) in dye-sensitive solar cells (DSSCs),15 CO2 reduction reaction (CO2RR) for direct conversion of CO2 into fuel,16 N2 reduction reaction (NRR) for the synthesis of NH3 under ambient environment,17 and for the renewable generation of clean energy from sunlight and water.18
Despite the remarkable progress, however, much more research efforts are still necessary to fundamentally understand the catalytic behaviors and realize practical applications of C-MFECs,5,8 particularly for some newly explored reactions (e.g., NRR, CO2RR, CO2ER, and H2O2PR). Herein, we present a timely, comprehensive, but critical review on recent advances in the field of C-MFECs with an emphasis on potential applications of C-MFECs for energy conversion and storage. In this article, various electrocatalytic reactions catalyzed by C-MFECs will be discussed and summarized. This is followed by detailed elucidation of the structure–property relationships for mechanistic understanding of C-MFECs, along with current challenges and future perspectives. Finally, among their many potential applications, the use of multifunctional C-MFECs in advanced energy devices and integrated energy systems is highlighted.
2. Carbon nanomaterials for electrocatalysis
2.1. C-MFECs based on carbon allotropes
As schematically shown in Fig. 2, C-MFECs primarily consist of carbon allotropes with different hybridization states of carbon–carbon bonds (e.g., sp3, sp2). Having different structural dimensions, ranging from 0 to 3D (0, 1, 2 and 3D), carbon allotropes can have different specific surface areas, conductivities, electrochemical activities, and mechanical properties (Fig. 3).4,5,10,19–23 0D carbon-based electrocatalysts include sp2 hybridized fullerene,24 carbon onions,25 quantum dots,26 and sp3 nanodiamond.27,28 While carbon nanotubes (CNTs), both single-walled (SWCNT) and multiwalled (MWCNT), and graphene are typical 1D and 2D C-MFECs, respectively, 3D C-MFECs are consist of disordered carbon foams and highly-ordered carbon pillars.20
 Fig. 2  Carbon-based allotropic nanomaterials from 0D fullerene through 1D CNTs and 2D graphene to 3D carbon architecture depending on hybridization states of C–C bonds. For 3D architecture, adapted with permission from ref. 20, copyright 2008 to American Chemical Society. The electronic, chemical, mechanical, and electrochemical properties of C-MFECs are determined by the dominant hybridization state of the carbon–carbon bonds, and the dopants/edge (e.g., armchair and zigzag edges)/defects (e.g., pentagon). Hybrids: nanotube–graphene pillars or porous carbons. 
 Fig. 3  Illustration of heteroatom doped C-MFECs. Upper panel: Different heteroatoms doped into the graphite carbon skeleton. Lower panel: Doping-induced charge-redistribution, spin redistribution, and charge-spin coupling based on the relative electronegativity of heteroatom dopants. Se, Br, and I are labeled with the superscript of *, denoting that these heteroatoms are too large to be incorporated into the carbon lattice and spatial distortion has to be considered as an important factor for inducing catalytic activities rather than pure contributions from charge and spin redistributions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 29, copyright 2019 to Wiley-VCH. 
Considerable recent efforts have been made to fabricate either ordered or disordered mesoporous 3D carbon materials via self-assembling (e.g., hydrothermal treatment followed by freeze-drying) of lower-dimensional carbon allotropes (Fig. 2).21,23 For electrocatalytic applications, 3D porous carbon nanomaterials possess multiple advantages, including a large surface area for enhanced exposure of the electrocatalytic active sites, the multidimensional conductive network for improved electron/ion transport with reliable mechanical stability, and high porosity for efficient electrolyte/reactant diffusion. However, it is difficult, if not impossible, for cost-efficient large-scale production of the highly ordered porous 3D carbon nanomaterials (e.g., pillared graphene) compared to the disordered 3D carbon foams.20,21
2.2. Physical characteristics of C-MFECs
For energy conversion and storage, it is highly desirable for C-MFECs to have high electrical and thermal conductivities for efficient charge transport and heat management.21 C-MFECs must also be mechanically stable and reliable to ensure a long-term operation life.21 Graphitic carbon allotropes with sp2 hybridization have been demonstrated to exhibit outstanding mechanical and conducting properties, as exemplified by graphene with an excellent electron mobility (200000 cm2 V−1 s−1), intrinsic conductivity (∼106 S m−1), and Young's modulus (∼1.0 TPa).21,30–32 Compared to the highly-ordered 3D carbon architectures of seamless junctions produced by CVD,33–36 however, the disordered 3D porous carbon materials (e.g., 3D graphene foams) are often made from self-assembling graphene oxide (GO) produced through acid oxidization of graphite flakes, followed by chemical reduction to form the reduced GO (designated as: rGO), leading to an electrical conductivity of only ∼0.1 to 2.98 × 104 S m−1 because of the undesired scattering of the charge carriers by the residual sp3 carbon bonded to oxygen in rGO.37
Generally speaking, CVD-grown carbon architectures possess a higher conductivity than their chemically-processed counterparts. Thus, the electrical conducting properties of carbon electrodes required for electrocatalytic applications can be enhanced by carbonization of C-MFECs at high temperatures (800–1000 °C).38,39 Indeed, N, S codoped porous carbon material synthesized by ball-milling of lyophilized melamine–nickel sulfate, followed by pyrolysis at 800 °C and acid etching (to remove the nickel species), was demonstrated to show an electrical conductivity of ca. 20–22 S m−1 with good trifunctional electrocatalytic activities.38
2.3. Electrocatalytic characteristics of C-MFECs

2.3.1 Multifunctional electrocatalytic activities for C-MFECs. 
To modulate the electrocatalytic activities of C-MFECs, various strategies, including heteroatom(s) doping and defect generation, have been developed.8,40 All of these strategies have been demonstrated to effectively modify the charge/spin distribution of the sp2-conjugated carbon networks (e.g., CNTs, graphene) or sp3-hybridized nanodiamonds. Specifically, the atomic sites with a positive charge and/or higher spin density can alter the chemisorption of the intermediates and the subsequent electron transfer for the break/formation of chemical bonds.8,29,40 While the modulated electron structures are favorable for effective adsorption of reaction intermediates, the valence orbital matching between the catalytic active site and the adsorbed reactant is considered to be important for facilitating electron transfer during the reaction.40 Furthermore, different atomic structures of dopants, defects, and edges (zigzag, armchair) can also contribute diversely to the electronic structure modulation, and hence tuning the electrocatalytic performance of C-MFECs.40
For heteroatom-doped C-MFECs, the doping process often redistributes charge density and/or spin density of carbon atoms in the lattice due to the difference in electronegativity/spin density between carbon atoms and heteroatoms, and thus effectively regulates the work function of the catalytic active centers (CACs) to enhance the adsorption of reactants at the CACs.29 Owing to the size difference between the carbon and dopant atoms, the local geometry in the proximity of heteroatom dopants also changed along with the doping/codoping/multidoping of heteroatom(s). When the large-size dopants, such as Se, Br, and I, are doped into the graphitic carbon lattice, the doping-induced spatial distortion could significantly enhance the catalytic activities. As schematically shown in Fig. 3, therefore, the electronegativity and atomic size of heteroatoms both play essential roles in regulating the catalytic performance of C-MFECs. For S-doped C-MFECs, however, charge redistribution is unlikely to occur due to the close electronegativity between sulfur (2.58) and carbon (2.55).41 Instead, the spin density redistribution induced by S-doping has been demonstrated to be responsible for the improved ORR catalytic activity.41–43 Moreover, such electronic and spin modulations can also be obtained by introducing lattice defects of atomic vacancies into C-MFECs as well as intermolecular charge-transfer and photo-induced charge-transfer over the whole pH range, as we should see below.
Intermolecular charge transfer for efficient ORR. 
As can be seen from the above discussion, doping graphitic carbon materials with heteroatoms and/or defects induced charge/spin redistributions to impart electrocatalytic activities to C-MFECs. In addition to the heteroatom(s)-doping-induced intramolecular charge-transfer, intermolecular charge transfer has also been reported to promote ORR and other reactions in C-MFECs.8 For instance, certain polyelectrolyte (e.g., PDDA) functionalized CNTs (either in an aligned or nonaligned form), and graphene were demonstrated to show efficient ORR electrocatalytic activities in an alkaline electrolyte (0.1 M KOH), along with OER and HER.44,45 Recently, the intermolecular charge-transfer between adsorbed C60 molecules and SWCNTs was reported to induce high catalytic activities for ORR in not only alkaline electrolyte (0.1 M KOH) with an onset potential of 0.91 V (vs. RHE, same below unless otherwise stated), but also neutral electrolyte (phosphate buffer).46Fig. 4a schematically shows the electrocatalytic mechanism for C60/SWCNTs, while Fig. 4b reproduces the corresponding ORR results.46
 Fig. 4  (a) Illustration of C60–SWCNTs for efficient trifunctional electrocatalysis, (b) linear sweep voltammetry curves (LSVs) pure C60, SWCNTs, C60–SWCNT15, and Pt/C towards ORR in 0.1 M KOH solution. Reproduced with permission from ref. 46, copyright 2019 to American Chemical Society. (c) The schematic representation of F/BCN nanohybrids for different catalytic reactions. (d) Illustration of the preparation of the EDC polyelectrolyte and EDC-modified CNTs as metal-free carbonaceous electrocatalysts, (e) RRDE polarization curves of the CNT–EDC, CNT–PDDA, CNT, and commercial Pt/C electrodes in O2-saturated 0.1 M KOH at a sweep rate of 5 mV s−1 with a speed rate of 1600 rpm. The ring potential: 1.5 V vs. RHE. Reproduced with permission from ref. 47, copyright 2019 to Wiley. 
In a similar but independent study, a new class of hybrid carbon-based materials composed of boron carbon nitride nanosheets and fullerene molecules (F/BCN) were reported as multifunctional catalysts for the HER/hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR) and the OER/ORR (Fig. 4c).48 Specifically, the remarkable ORR electrocatalytic properties of F/BCN (especially for 10 wt% fullerene in BCN nanosheets, 10% F/BCN) are associated with the positively charged N active sites at the interfaces between C60 and BCN sheets, the large amount of pyridinic N atoms in the BCN nanosheets of 10% F/BCN as Lewis base sites for the oxygen adsorption, and the possible influence of the “B–C–N” edge groups.48 Based on the calculated Gibbs free energy, the 10% F/BCN showed the low rate-limiting potential (0.81 eV), thus decreasing the energy barrier for the rate-determining catalytic step. A desirable low value of the potential (0.69 eV) of the limiting step (*O → *OOH) was calculated for OER, the local interactions as well as the intermolecular electron transfer between the fullerenes and the BCN nanosheets for the 10% F/BCN system decreased the uphill energy states toward the formation of catalytic intermediates, thus leading to the good catalytic activity towards OER. The value of hydrogen adsorption (ΔGH*) is 0.03 eV for 10% F/BCN, which is very close to the ideal value of zero and in agreement with the superior HER performance. In addition, the 10% F/BCN catalyst generated the high current density of 1.47 mA cm−2 at 0.1 V vs. RHE for HOR, in good consistence with the theoretical ΔGH* value of −0.03 eV. Thus, the 10% F/BCN can act as a multifunctional C-MFEC towards ORR/OER/HER/HOR.48
In another study, CNTs modified by epichlorohydrin-dimethylamine copolymer (EDC) were also demonstrated to show intermolecular charge (electron) transfer from the CNT to EDC to serve as a C-MFEC with remarkable catalytic activity and stability (Fig. 4d and e).47 As shown in Fig. 4e, the CNT–EDC exhibited an ORR onset potential of 0.87 V and half-wave potential of 0.70 V with a large diffusion-limited current density of 3.52 mA cm−2 in 0.1 M KOH electrolyte.47 Moreover, nitrobenzene groups have also been chemically adsorbed onto a graphene sheet to act as a p-type dopant for charge transfer with the graphene sheet, leading to a higher ORR activity than that of the pristine graphene.49 Thus, it can be envisioned that a proper match of the donor–acceptor pair for an efficient intermolecular charge-transfer could make the intermolecular charge-transfer route an effective approach for cost-effective mass production of high-performance C-MFECs. More research activities in this direction would be of great value.
The above heteroatom-/defect-free methodology provided a facile, efficient, and cost-effective way to the development of metal-free, multifunctional catalysts simply by adsorbing electron-acceptors with electron-donors. The methodology thus developed is simpler than the heteroatom-/defect-doping routes toward C-MFECs, which usually involve the energy-consuming pyrolysis process. Furthermore, heteroatom-/defect-free C-MFECs are prepared by adsorption, which causes no structural damage of the carbon materials to retain the structure integrity and good property of the constituents, along with a good stability for the resultant C-MFEC. In contrast, doping heteroatoms into the carbon skeleton often cause structure damage, leading to a poor catalytic stability.
Photo-enhanced ORR. 
Due to the photo-induced charge-transfer property intrinsically associated with C60 molecule, preliminary results have also demonstrated that C60/SWCNTs possess photo-induced electrocatalytic activities for ORR.46 In this context, g-C3N4 has also been reported to act as both a photon absorber and electrocatalyst for ORR.50 As such, g-C3N4 with proper band-gap engineering can be utilized for photo-induced ORR.50,51 Doping with non-metal elements is an effective way to tune the redox potentials (band-gap) and the photogenerated charge transport/transfer efficiency for g-C3N4.52 Furthermore, O-doping of g-C3N4 can restrain the recombination of electron–hole pairs and enhance the light absorption efficiency53 while doping with the elements of a large atomic number (S, Se) can weaken the O2 adsorption.54 Indeed, the ORR performance of g-C3N4 was reported to increase under visible light illumination, though detailed mechanistic understanding is needed.54
pH independent ORR. 
It is necessary for C-MFECs to show efficient ORR performance in various electrolytes having different pH values so that they could be used either in an alkaline or an acid electrolyte and be readily integrated into efficient devices/systems with a single electrolyte. As it is well known, most of the transition-/noble-metal-based ORR electrocatalysts are not stable in acidic electrolytes for long-term operation.55 C-MFECs are promising for such utilizations because of their structural diversity and excellent stability in different electrolytes. So far, a few pH-universal C-MFECs have been devised.56–61 Of particular interest, P/N-codoped porous carbon foams (P–N-GFs-HMPA) were prepared by solvothermal method, followed by pyrolysis utilizing graphene oxides and carbon nanotubes as carbon building blocks and hexamethylphosphoric triamide (HMPA) as the P and N sources.58 Interestingly, the use of HMPA could not only remarkably increase the content of both P and N, but also offer a high density of the coupled N–P moieties. This, together with the optimized open-porous local structures for efficient electron and electrolyte transports, makes the resultant P–N-GFs-HMPA foam exhibit excellent electrocatalytic performance for ORR in acidic medium (0.1 M HClO4), having an E1/2 of about 0.78 V (0.82 V for Pt/C) and onset potential of about 0.98 V (0.93 V for Pt/C) with a limiting current density of 4.7 mA cm−2 (4.9 mA cm−2 for Pt/C). This electrocatalyst showed an onset potential of 1.04 V for ORR in an alkaline electrolyte (0.1 M KOH), which is also superior to the Pt/C catalyst.58 These results indicated that highly efficient pH-universal C-MFECs for ORR electrocatalysis could be developed by proper heteroatom(s) doping and multidimensional structure engineering.
In view of the above discussions, therefore, electrocatalytic activities induced by the charge-/spin-redistribution (both intramolecular and intermolecular) via heteroatom-/defect-doping, spin-charge coupling, photo-irradiation, and pH-variation should be investigated further by combined experimental and theoretical approaches to identify CACs for specific electrochemical reactions. Insights thus gained will be used to guide the design and development of advanced multifunctional C-MFECs for integrated energy conversion and storage systems of practical significance.8,18,29,40,43,62
By integrating multiple CACs with specific functions through multi-doping with more than one kind of heteroatoms, multifunctional C-MFECs can be created for multiple electron transfer reactions (e.g., ORR, OER, HER, CO2RR, NRR).21,63 Unlike metal alloys that often suffer segregation problems, the heteroatom-doped multifunctional C-MFECs with covalent chemical bonds between the carbon base and dopant atoms should have no segregation issue, leading to good stability. Apart from the cost and stability benefits, multifunctional C-MFECs offer multiple advantages over traditional metal-based electrocatalysts for broad applications, particularly for integrated energy conversion and storage.18,62 However, identifying the intrinsic catalytic activities and the structures associated with each of the multiple CACs in a multifunctional C-MFEC is a challenging task. Further research work in this emerging area is needed.


2.3.2 Electrocatalytic active sites for heteroatom-/defect-doped C-MFECs. 
As briefly mentioned above, one of the powerful approaches to acquire high electrocatalytic activities for carbon materials is to dope/co-dope/multi-dope them with different heteroatom(s), such as nitrogen (N), sulphur (S), boron (B), phosphorus (P), fluorine (F) and oxygen (O).21,63 Upon doping with heteroatom(s), the C atoms adjacent to heteroatom dopants can become either negatively or positively charged, depending on their electronegativity mismatches and relative positions (Fig. 5a).64 Among many physical and chemical factors, including the effective surface area, availability of active reaction centers on the catalyst surface, electronic and ionic conductivities, stability in various electrolytes, and mechanical reliability, the doping-induced charge redistribution is the crucial factor to determine the chemical selectivity by regulating the adsorption of reactants/intermediates and the associated charge-transfer between the catalytic surface and absorbents, and hence the ultimate electrocatalytic performance of C-MFECs.21
 Fig. 5  The Mulliken population charges on the doped atoms and the atoms around the doped atom in graphene lattice: (a) are for undoped, BC−, NC−, SC−, and PC+ doped graphene, respectively. Depending on the electronegativity difference, the C atom acquired −ve or +ve charges. Reproduced with permission from ref. 64. (b) The doped “N” configurations in graphitic carbon. Reproduced with permission from ref. 65. (c) Comparative LSV curves using N-HOPG model catalysts for different N configurations, showing pyridinic N as active sites towards ORR in 0.1 M H2SO4. Adapted with permission from ref. 66, copyright 2016 to American Association for the Advancement of Science. 
Among the C-MFECs doped with various heteroatoms, N-doped C-MFECs have been most extensively studied. Now, it is well known that N dopants can form different configurations within the graphitic carbon lattice, such as pyridinic-N, pyrrolic-N, graphitic-N, and oxidized-N.65Fig. 5b shows these N-configurations along with the corresponding binding energies obtained from X-ray photoelectron spectroscopic (XPS) analyses.65 For the design of efficient N-doped C-MFECs, it is essential to understand correlations between the specific nitrogen-binding configurations and the electrocatalytic activities for a particular reaction.66 Using HOPG model catalysts and nitrogen-doped graphene nanosheets (N-GNS) powder catalysts, Guo et al., have recently revealed that C-atoms next to the pyridinic N with Lewis basicity are the active sites for ORR in acidic electrolyte (0.1 M H2SO4).66 As shown in Fig. 5c, the LSV curves from HOPG samples with different contents of pyridinic N and graphitic N atoms confirmed the pyridinic N atoms as the active sites for ORR in an acidic electrolyte (0.1 M H2SO4).66 In another similar but independent recent study, Yao and co-workers have revealed that the zigzag lattice edge and the pentagonal Stone–Wales defect at the carbon lattice edge could also exhibit efficient ORR activities in acidic electrolyte.67 Although significant progress has been achieved, it is still difficult to distinguish contributions from the heteroatom(s), vacancy, or Stone–Wales defect for a specific C-MFEC as heteroatom-doping often introduces defects and/or vacancies. Furthermore, highly accurate methodologies to simultaneously control and probe the atomic-level location, structure, and chemistries of CACs remain elusive.68 It is necessary to integrate the synthesis, characterization, and modeling to elucidate the fundamental issues that control catalytic activities, and to track the dynamic material evolution in the reaction environments in situ. Insights thus gained could be used to guide the design and fabrication of advanced C-MFECs of practical significance.


2.3.3 Design and synthesis of efficient C-MFECs. 
The electrocatalytic performance of C-MFECs is highly dependent on the availability of active sites and their interactions with reactant/product/intermediate molecules and electrolyte ions. The activity of active sites varies with synthesis and processing conditions.21,69,70 For instance, chemically synthesized C-MFECs often need pyrolysis at high temperatures (700 to 1000 °C) for graphitization/formation of micro/mesopores, and the temperature at which the final product is annealed influence the electrocatalytic performance. It was demonstrated that annealing at 1000 °C is better than 900 °C for pH universal ORR catalyzed by chemically synthesized N/P-codoped graphitic carbon materials,57 while pyrolysis at 700 (for 2 h), 800 (for 2 h) and 900 °C (for 1 h) consecutively in Ar gas flow was reported to be effective for S/N-codoped graphite sheets with stereoscopic holes towards trifunctional (ORR–OER–HER) electrocatalysis.38
N/P/F-tridoped graphene sheets were also produced by pyrolyzing a mixture of GO, polyaniline, and ammonium hexafluorophosphate at 950 °C for 1 h for trifunctional ORR–OER–HER.62 Besides, a mesoporous carbon foam co-doped with N and P (NPMC), fabricated using a scalable, one-step process involving the polyaniline aerogel synthesized in the presence of phytic acid, followed by pyrolysis at different temperature (900 to 1100 °C, Fig. 6a), showed good bifunctional electrocatalytic properties for both ORR and OER.71 Although a larger surface area of ∼1663 m2 g−1 was obtained for NPMC-1100 in comparison to NPMC-1000 (surface area: 1548 m2 g−1), NPMC-1000 exhibited much better bifunctional electrocatalytic performance due to the decomposition of heteroatoms at 1100 °C.71 Thus, electrocatalytic activities of the C-MFECs depend on not only the surface area but also many other factors, such as pyrolysis temperature, dopant amount, conductivity, and stability.8
 Fig. 6  (a) The preparation process for the NPMC foams. A three-dimensional polyaniline (PANi) hydrogel crosslinked with phytic acids was formed by oxidative polymerization of aniline and phytic acid. (i) Aniline; (ii) phytic acid; (iii) complex. The PANi hydrogel is converted into aerogel freeze-dried into an aerogel and pyrolyzed in Ar to generate the NPMC. Reproduced with permission from ref. 71, copyright 2015 to Springer Nature. (b) Schematic illustration and (c) their reaction mechanism depicting the development of the holey S-C2NA with 3D structure. Reproduced with permission from ref. 72, copyright 2018 to American Chemical Society. 
Chemical synthesis and polymer carbonization. 
It was demonstrated that N-doping occurred at a temperature as low as 80 °C and up to 1100 °C for rGO by hydrothermal or thermal treatment of GO with urea/hydrazine or ammonia, showing the highest N-doping level at 500 °C.73–76 Generally speaking, heteroatom(s) doping in GO is a complicate process, which depends on its morphology, oxidation level, and nature of the functional groups.74–76 The thermal treatment above 900 °C generally results in the decomposition of some heteroatoms from the carbon skeleton.77 However, it is still an issue of controversy as annealing at 900 °C or above is often used to remove chemical residues and enhance the crystallinity and conductivity for C-MFECs obtained by chemical or hydrothermal methods.21,38 Furthermore, chemically fabricated 3D C-MFECs typically demonstrate a lower specific surface area and an inferior conductivity, along with the agglomeration of carbon nanosheets into stacked graphitic structures to further reduce electrocatalytic activities, making them less efficient than 3D C-MFECs produced by chemical vapor deposition (CVD, vide infra).21 However, chemical processes are relatively simple and cost-effective for a large-scale production of C-MFECs for commercial applications.
Efficient C-MFECs can also be chemically synthesized from various polymers in large quantities at a low cost. In particular, the use of controlled polymerization methods, such as atom transfer radical polymerization and reversible addition–fragmentation chain transfer polymerization, enabled the synthesis of polymers with controlled degrees of polymerization (DP), low dispersity, and complex but well-defined monomer sequence arrangements and architectures.78 In fact, well-defined polymeric precursors from controlled polymerization are very useful, especially those with different architectures that can be used for chain alignment, determination of the types of exposed surfaces, and/or control the size of conjugated domains.78–82 Polymer-based precursors that easily form carbon materials generally need to undergo a stabilization step (e.g., crosslinking) that can occur thermally, chemically, or their combination before reaching to pyrolytic temperatures.79–81 These stabilization reactions in many ways determine how effectively a polymer can be converted into carbon.78,82 In general, the activity of N-doped nanocarbons derived from polyacrylonitrile (PAN) is related to two aspects of their electronic structure: (i) the synergistic interaction of frontier π (nonbonding electrons on zigzag edges of nanographitic domains) and σ (lone pairs of zigzag edge nitrogen elements) orbitals, and (ii) the propensity of extended polyaromatic systems toward open-shell states.78–83 The role of (i) is related to the nonbonding and radical character of zigzag π electrons, which places them at the Fermi energy and enhances their ability to participate in reduction reactions (e.g., ORR, HER), and the potential for lone pairs of zigzag-edge N-atoms to act as binding/coordination sites for reaction intermediates. The role of (ii) relates to the tendency of extended polyaromatic systems to develop open-shell states along their zigzag edges, which could lead to the tendency of spin polarized electronic density to accumulate at zigzag edges.
Synthetic polymers now represent a major class of precursors for developing cutting-edge porous carbons. Among them, conjugated polymers have emerged as an attractive family of carbon precursors84 as their robust conjugated structure ensures a stable and efficient transformation of designed polymer architectures into desirable carbon nanostructures. Moreover, the heteroatom(s) (e.g., N, S, B, F, and P) dopants can be integrated into the carbon framework of C-MFECs directly by introducing the heteroatom-containing monomers for polymerization.71,84–87 As such, precise control of the composition and structure of C-MFECs at the molecular level are possible by using structurally well-defined conjugated porous networks (e.g., conjugated microporous polymers, covalent organic frameworks) as the polymer precursors.85 In these cases, polymerization is generally followed by carbonization through pyrolysis at high temperatures.71,88
For large-scale production at a lower cost, however, C-MFECs fabricated by low-temperature polymer processing are attractive. In this regard, hierarchical 3D sulfur-modulated holey C2N aerogels (C2NA) were fabricated by a simple amination and polymerization of chloroanilic acid through a template-free scalable method, followed by a thermal reduction process at 200 °C.72Fig. 6b and c schematically shows procedures for the preparation of the 3D holey S–C2NA bifunctional catalyst, along with the morphological details (Fig. 6d).72 The resultant S–C2NA exhibited an ultrahigh surface area of 1943 m2 g−1 and superb electrocatalytic activities (with an onset potential of 0.98 V and a limiting current density of 6.6 mA cm−2) towards ORR in 0.1 M KOH, which is superior to commercial Pt/C (with an overpotential of 0.96 V and a limiting current density of 6.0 3 mA cm−2) catalyst. Furthermore, this S-C2N aerogel demonstrated an overpotential of 0.3 V at 10 mA cm−2 current density for OER (0.1 M KOH), which is even better than the state-of-the-art OER catalyst (i.e., RuO2, 0.32 V). Besides polymers, carbon-based materials have also been fabricated at low temperatures from ionic liquids, ionomers, glucose, and other precursors.89–97 To achieve commercialization success. However, the conductivity of low-temperature generated carbon materials need to be further improved as is their mechanical resilience.
Metal–organic framework derived C-MFECs. 
Recently, metal–organic framework (MOF) derived C-MFECs have also been demonstrated to have a high specific surface area and efficient electrocatalytic performance.98 Various MOFs obtained from metal ions, and polyfunctional organic ligands have been proved to be ideal sacrificial templates for fabricating various carbon-based nanomaterials,99 including porous carbons and heteroatom doped porous carbons via thermal decomposition under controlled atmospheres.98–104 The C-MFECs prepared by MOF-templated precursors possess multiple advantages, including a high specific surface area, tailorable porosity, diversity in ordered 3D carbon architectures, and easy functionalization with other heteroatoms or metal/metal oxides to replace precious and nonprecious metal-based electrodes for electrocatalysis.99–101 For C-MFECs derived from MOF, the sublimation of Zinc species or acid treatment process are involved to remove the metal species. For instance, nitrogen-doped porous carbon materials (NPC-1000-ts) produced simply by pyrolyzing bio-MOF-1 precursor (Fig. 7a) outperformed the commercial Pt/C catalyst for ORR in an alkaline electrolyte (0.1 M KOH) in terms of overpotential, limiting current density, and long term stability,102 prompting more research activities along this direction.
 Fig. 7  (a) Synthesis procedures of NPC from MOF along with its structural details. Reproduced with permission from ref. 102, copyright 2019 to Elsevier Inc. (b) Schematic for the fabrication of the 3D g-C3N4NS–CNT porous composite. (c) SEM image of g-C3N4 NS–CNT. Reproduced with permission from ref. 105, copyright 2014 to American Chemical Society. 
Self-assembling. 
Self-assembling driven by π–π stacking or electrostatic interactions has been proven another effective route to efficient C-MFECs. In particular, a 3D interconnected carbon composite was prepared by low-temperature self-assembling g-C3N4 nanosheets (g-C3N4 NSs) and CNTs into g-C3N4 NS-CNT (Fig. 7b and c), which exhibited comparable OER performance (onset potential = 1.53 V vs. RHE in 0.1 M KOH) with more favorable kinetics and better durability than noble-metal based catalysts (e.g., IrO2–CNT and derivatives).105
In other investigations, C-MFECs based on 3D graphene assemblies have been developed.106,107 Of particular interest, a stable aqueous dispersion of polymer-modified graphene sheets was prepared via in situ reduction of exfoliated graphite oxides in the presence of cationic poly(ethyleneimine) (PEI).106 The resultant water-soluble PEI-modified graphene sheets were then used for sequential self-assembly with acid-oxidized multiwalled CNTs, forming hybrid carbon films with an interconnected network of carbon structures with well-defined nanopores, which have great potential as C-MFECs. As mentioned earlier, C60 has also been adsorbed (self-assembled) onto SWCNTs as an electron acceptor to induce intermolecular charge-transfer with the SWCNTs, forming a class of new C60-SWCNT based C-MFECs with photo-responsive trifunctional electrocatalytic activities for ORR, OER, and HER over a whole pH range (cf.Fig. 4a and b).46
Chemical vapor deposition (CVD). 
Like conventional catalysts, the catalytic activities of C-MFECs are controlled by the intrinsic catalytic activity of individual catalytic active centres (CACs), such as their dopant type, active domain size, and strength to interact with reactants, and also by the surrounding environments and the density of CACs within the catalytic unit and substrate architectures. The design of 3D porous structures, where more CACs are exposed for participation in catalytic reactions can further improve catalytic activities. Thus, high-performance C-MFECs could be achieved by not only precisely controlling the type and size of each specific CAC, but also by controlling the microstructures of the catalyst itself and/or its support (e.g., 3-D architecture, porosity) to enhance the density and distribution of the CACs within the overall C-MFEC. In this context, an innovative strategy of fabricating 3D graphene–carbon nanotube (CNT) hollow fibers with radially aligned CNTs (RACNTs) seamlessly sheathed by a cylindrical graphene layer was conceived through a one-step CVD using an anodized aluminum wire template (Fig. 8a).36 By controlling the diameter of aluminum wire and anodization time, the length of the RACNTs and diameter of the graphene hollow fiber were well-tuned, enabling efficient energy conversion and storage. The RACNTs were connected to a graphene sheet of homogeneously distributed open holes, as shown by the TEM images in Fig. 8b–g. These fibers, with a controllable surface area, meso-/micropores, and superior electrical properties, demonstrated excellent counter-electrode properties in dye-sensitized solar cells, having a power conversion efficiency of 6.8% and outperformed their counterpart with an expensive Pt wire counter electrode by a factor of 2.5 (2.74% efficiency).36
 Fig. 8  (a) Schematic diagrams of the synthesis and microstructures of a 3D graphene–RACNT fiber. (b and c) Graphene sheet connecting to the open tips of RACNTs, (d) closed-end of RACNTs, (e) TEM image of the side view of the 3D graphene–RACNT around the graphene-nanotube interface, and (f and g) cross-section view of the constituent RACNTs within the 3D graphene–RACNT structure. Adapted from ref. 36, copyright 2015 to American Association for the Advancement of Science. (h) Schematic illustration for the synthesis of aligned CNT/graphene sandwiches by two-step thermal CVD method, (i and j) SEM images of the aligned CNT/graphene composite, (k) TEM images of the sandwich sample prepared via protensive ultrasonic treatment. Multi-walled graphitic rings were observed, and the structure was further characterized by high-resolution TEM (inset), showing a notch (arrowed) on the ring, (l and m) SEM images of the aligned CNT/graphene sandwiches, (m) Continuous graphene sheets at the bottom of aligned CNTs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 108, copyright 2014 to Elsevier Inc. (n) Schematic illustration of catalytic CVD of graphene (G)/SWCNT hybrids on layered double hydroxide (LDH) flakes, (o) HRTEM image of the G/SWCNT hybrids showing the interface between SWCNTs and graphene. Reproduced with permission from ref. 109, copyright 2012 to American Chemical Society. (p) SEM image of CNT/FLG hybrid structures by fabricated by PECVD graphene growth on MWCNTs without any catalysts, (q and r) TEM and HR-TEM images of FLGs on CNTs. Adapted with permission from ref. 110, copyright 2011 to American Chemical Society. 
To realize a mechanically resilient and robust 3D carbon architecture, researchers effectively sandwiched CNTs and graphene into freestanding sp2 all-carbon hybrids, rendering the excellent loading transfer in 3D framework (Fig. 8h–m).108 The millimeter-scale aligned CNT/graphene sandwiches could be repeatedly compressed at high strains (ε ∼ 490%), with a highest energy absorption density of 237.1 kJ kg−1, an ultrahigh power density of 10.4 kW kg−1, and remarkable efficiency of 83% during steady operation, providing novel nanocomposites with outstanding mechanical energy storage performance and promising to electrochemical catalytic applications.108 It was found that the roots of aligned, which anchored on the graphene sheets, provided such excellent loading transfer between graphene and CNTs at high-stress loading, and the compressive modulus could reach as high as 2.3 GPa before the collapse of the structure, exhibiting superiority over other conventional material like steel.108 Furthermore, seamless and covalently bonded 3D graphene/CNT hybrid material was also fabricated with the aid of an Al2O3 as a floating buffer layer in CVD.111 The CVD-generated sp2 graphene/CNT interlinked networks have an electrical conductivity of 29.3–43.4 S cm−1, which is attractive for electrocatalytic applications.109,111,112
On the other hand, one-step growth of CNTs on both sides of graphene (G/CNT hybrid) was achieved on FeMgAl layered double hydroxide (LDH) sheets at a high temperature over 950 °C.110 In this particular case, the thermally stable Fe nanoparticles and the uniform structure of the calcined LDH flakes were essential for the simultaneously catalytic deposition of single-wall CNTs (SWCNTs) and graphene (Fig. 8n and o). The robust connection between the CVD-grown SWCNTs and graphene ensured a high electrically conductive pathway for efficient electrocatalysis. Moreover, 3D carbon nanostructure comprising few-layer graphene (FLG) sheets inherently connected with CNTs through sp2 carbons, resembling plant leaves (FLGs) growing on stems (CNTs) was also synthesized by plasma-enhanced CVD (PECVD), as shown in Fig. 8p–r.110 Unlike a random mixture of CNTs and graphene sheets (CNT/G) with poor CNT/graphene interfacial contacts, this CNT/FLG structure has intrinsic chemical bonding between the two constituent components with low interfacial resistances attractive for C-MFEC applications. Other carbon-supported vertically-oriented carbon nanostructures, such as MWCNTs and carbon foams, have also been prepared as potential C-MFECs by microwave plasma-enhanced CVD.113–115
As CNT–graphene hybrids hold great promise for C-MFECs, a large variety of growth methodologies have been reported to develop CNT–graphene hybrids via intermolecular induced intersections between the individual CNTs and graphene sheets.116–119 Also, the CVD approach is often used for simultaneous heteroatom-doping during the carbon growth,21 though CVD-synthesis of controllable ordered CNT–graphene porous hybrids is still challenging.21,112,116–118 It is evident that the trend to use CNT–graphene hybrids with multidimensionalities and multifunctionalities as C-MFECs will continue.
Ball milling. 
As mentioned above, the heteroatom(s) doping and defect(s) incorporation in carbon skeletons of C-MFECs can impart electrocatalytic activities for efficient energy conversion and storage. However, it is also well known that the heteroatoms and/or defects presented in graphitic carbon lattices act as scattering centers for charge carriers (electrons, ions) to reduce the conductivity and hence the electrocatalytic activity.8,120 To address this issue, edge-doped graphitic carbon sheets with heteroatom(s) and/or defect(s) selectively at the edges have been developed to keep the basal plane dopant- or defect-free for retaining their excellent conducting properties. Ball milling has been widely used for such selective edge engineering,120–122 which is basically a mechanochemical method that can be used for simultaneous exfoliation of graphite flakes and doping the edges of graphene nanoplatelets (less than 10 graphene layers). Fig. 9 schematically shows the ball milling method to produce edge-functionalized graphene nanoplatelets (EFGnPs) from the pristine graphite in the presence of the target dopants (X = nonmetals, halogens, semimetals, or metalloids).121 Specifically, the electron accepting/donating properties of the heteroatoms in EFGnPs can generate variations of the overall electron-charge densities on neighboring carbon atoms to promote electrocatalytic activities effectively. The as-prepared EFGnPs with superior characteristics have shown excellent performance in various applications, including fuel cells, solar cells, supercapacitors, Li-ion batteries (LIBs), vanadium redox-flow batteries (VRFBs), flame retardants, to mention a few.123–129
 Fig. 9  (a and b) Digital photo of ball-mill machine and a cross-sectional view of the ball-mill reactor. (c) Mechanochemically induced chemical reaction via ball milling of graphite in the presence of target X substance(s) and subsequent workup procedure to produce EFGnPs with desired functional groups. (d) Edge functionalization of EFGnPs with different heteroatoms. Adapted from ref. 121 under creative commons license, copyright 2019 to Wiley. 

3. C-MFECs for various electrocatalytic reactions
3.1. Recent advances in C-MFECs for ORR
C-MFECs have been most extensively researched for ORR, and tremendous progress has been achieved since the first C-MFECs (i.e., N-doped CNT) was discovered for efficient ORR performance in 2009.6 Since then, various C-MFECs, including CNTs, graphene, graphene foams, graphene quantum dots (GQD), edge doped graphene flakes, mesoporous carbons, graphene–CNT based hybrids, carbon onions, carbon nano-cones, graphene nanoribbons, carbon cages, hollow carbons, and even nanodiamonds doped, codoped or tridoped with N, S, B, F, P, Se, and/or O, have been demonstrated to show ORR electrocatalytic properties comparable to or even better than those of the Pt-based commercial catalyst, in terms of overpotential, onset potential, limiting current density, and long-term stability.4,5,10,21,63 Examples include, but not limited to, B/N-codoped C-aerogels,130 defect-rich-N-carbons,131 biomass-derived mesoporous carbons,132–135 N/F/S-tridoped graphene,136 N-hierarchically porous hollow C-tubes,137 N and P co-doped hollow carbon spheres,138 N-rich C-onion,139 MOF-derived heteroatom(s) doped porous carbons,140 covalent organic framework derived N/P-codoped carbons,141 supramolecular assisted synthesized N-3D carbons,142 CNTs encapsulated in N/P-codoped carbons,143 zigzag edged graphene nanoribbons,144 hard-template derived S/N-codoped porous carbons,145 B/N-codoped porous carbons,146 oxidizing-laser induced graphene,147 oxygen doped g-C3N4 for photo-enhanced ORR,54 N-doped carbon nanotube arrays on carbon fiber cloth,148 N-doped holey carbon/graphene hybrids,149 N-doped interconnected C-web,150 S–rGO,151 porous B/N-sheets,152 N/P-codoped porous carbon networks,153 polymer-derived N/P-codoped mesoporous carbons,154 B-GQDs,155 S/N-codoped holey carbon sheets,38 CNT-graphene based hybrid 3D nanostructured carbons,156 3D aerogel containing N-holey carbon sheets,72 N/P-codoped mesoporous C sheets,57 and carbide-derived N-carbon.157 In particular, critical efforts have been made to develop C-MFECs for ORR over a wide range of pH values and to achieve a mechanistic understanding of the CACs to guide the design and development of efficient C-MFECs of practical significance for reducing/replacing metal-based ORR electrocatalysts.130–159 The materials, preparation methods, and the specific performance of the reported C-MECs for ORR are summarized in Table 1. Since the research and development of C-MFECs for ORR have been comprehensively reviewed previously,5 only recent advances are addressed below.

3.1.1. Recent advances in morphological designing for ORR. 
As mentioned above, the use of advanced materials design and synthesis to control and integrate CACs into 3D porous carbon architectures is critical to strengthen the catalytic activities of C-MFECs. To achieve this goal, nitrogen-enriched aromatic polymers and colloidal silica with different diameter were employed as precursor and hard templates, respectively, to produce the N doped porous carbon nanospheres with tunable diameters and pore sizes. The controlled self-assembly behavior of colloidal silica enables the generation of assembled carbon nanospheres with hierarchical structure after the removal of the silica for substantially improving the accessibility of activity sites during electrochemical catalysis. The above resultant carbon-based materials, having a high surface area, suitable pore volume, and numerous N-induced active sites, showed the high ORR activity in alkaline media.166–168 The ideal C-MFECs promising for large-scale production have also been prepared by combining two strategies: the use of a 2D covalent organic framework (COF) and the development of a suitable template to guide the pyrolysis at 1000 °C (Fig. 10a–c).141 By this approach, nanosized 2D graphitic carbon sheets (PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000) with high conductivity, hierarchical micropores/mesopores, and abundant N-/P-doped heteroatom catalytic edges were generated. The resultant PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000 catalyst showed a lower onset potential, and hence better ORR performance than that of the Pt/C. Subsequent annealing in NH3 atmosphere at 900 °C doubled its surface area from 495 to 1160 m2 g−1, and further enhanced the ORR performance for the (PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000NH3) catalyst over the Pt/C in terms of the onset potential (0 V vs. −0.03 V) vs. Ag/AgCl, half-wave potentials (−0.11 V vs. −0.16 V) vs. Ag/AgCl, limit current density (7.2 mA cm−2vs. 6.0 mA cm−2), Tafel slope (110 mV dec−1vs. 121 mV dec−1), long-time stability, and methanol tolerance in 0.1 M KOH electrolyte, as exemplified by Fig. 10d. The PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000NH3 also exhibited fantastic OER electrocatalysis in 0.1 M KOH solution, attaining a current density of −10 mA cm−2 at only 0.97 V vs. Ag/AgCl that is one order of magnitude higher than that of the Pt/C (1.01 mA cm−2). The observed excellent electrocatalytic properties could be attributed to its special morphology, mesoporous structure, high conductivity, and the presence of heteroatoms (N and P) containing graphitic carbon edges. These results clearly demonstrated that rational structural design could lead to advanced ORR electrocatalysis by C-MFECs.
 Fig. 10  (a and b) Detailed structure of the template and the synthesis procedure of TAPT-DHTA-COF. (c) The conversion of COFs into carbons by template or non-template pyrolysis. (d) ORR polarization curves of TAPT–DHTA-COF1000 (green), PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000 (blue), PA@TAPT–DHTA-COF1000NH3 (red), and Pt/C (black) electrodes in O2-saturated 0.1 M KOH solution with speed rate of 1600 rpm. Adapted with permission from ref. 163, copyright 2018 to Springer Nature. 
Another typical example for structural modulation induced advancement in ORR performance is graphdiyne (GDY), a two-dimensional carbon allotrope featured by its sp- and sp2-hybridized carbon atoms (Fig. 10e).160 The N-doped GDY was synthesized with pyridine and NH3 as the N sources and showed an electrocatalytic activity at a potential above −0.8 V, similar to that of the commercial Pt/C, towards ORR in alkaline solution with a higher stability and better methanol tolerance than those of Pt/C.161,162 More recently, N-doped few-layer graphdiyne (NFLGDY) was also synthesized with the N-doping moieties in the form of sp-hybridized N (sp-N) atoms (Fig. 10a), through pericyclic replacement of the acetylene groups.163 This method allowed for specific control of the doping site and proportion of sp-N atoms. As shown in Fig. 10f, XPS spectra confirmed the sp-N doping. Combined experimental and theoretical studies indicated that the site-specific sp-N atoms are beneficial for O2 adsorption for electrocatalytic ORR in 0.1 M KOH electrolyte.163 The optimal sp-N-doped FLGDY exhibited a catalytic activity comparable to that of the Pt/C, with a half-wave potential (E1/2) of 0.87 V (0.86 V for Pt/C) and kinetic current density (Jk) of 38.0 mA cm−2 at 0.75 V in 0.1 M KOH, as well as better stability and methanol resistance than those of the Pt/C in both alkaline (0.1 M KOH) and acidic (0.1 M HClO4) solutions (Fig. 10g).163 This strategy to incorporate sp-N atoms into carbon materials in a controllable manner could open new opportunities for site-specific doping of other C-MFECs.


3.1.2. Recent advances in edge engineering for ORR. 
The edges and/or lattice-grain boundaries on graphitic carbons generally possess unpaired dangling bonds, adsorbed active functional groups, and/or trapped charges, facilitating charge transfer to improve the ORR performance.169–175 The enhanced electrocatalytic activity at graphitic carbon edges has been demonstrated by micro-electrochemical testing systems developed to measure the ORR activities precisely at the edge and basal-plane regions on the surface of freshly exfoliated HOPG sheets,169 Ar plasma-treated edge-rich carbon nanomaterials (graphene and CNTs),170 and the edge and basal selectively exposed graphitic layers.171,172 Exposed monolayer graphene edge demonstrated ORR activity almost 2 orders of magnitude higher than that of the graphene basal plane in 0.1 M PBS solution containing 0.1 M KCl.171 Furthermore, chemical and/or physical doping heteroatoms at the graphitic lattice edges have been demonstrated to enhance the ORR electrocatalysis.41,174 For instance, edge-selectively sulfurized graphene nanoplatelets, obtained by ball milling graphite and sulfur powders together, exhibited about 4 times higher ORR electrocatalytic activities over the pristine graphite powder in 0.1 M KOH solution.41 Theoretical calculations have revealed that C–C bond distortions in the carbon skeleton could lead to strong O2 binding while spin density distribution along the B/N-codoped carbon edges plays a key role in enhancing the adsorption strength of O2 to facilitate the ORR electrocatalysis.176
More recently, Shui and co-workers have demonstrated that the zigzag edges of graphitic carbon lattices are more active than the armchair edges (Fig. 11a–d). In this particular case, MWCNTs were partially unzipped to obtain zigzag-edged graphene nanoribbons with N doping and a carbon nanotube backbone (N-GNR@CNT) for ORR in proton exchange membrane fuel cells (Fig. 11e and f), showing a peak areal power density of 0.161 W cm−2 and a peak mass power density of 520 W g−1 superior to most non-precious-metal electrocatalysts.144 Furthermore, the zigzag carbon showed stability better than a representative iron–nitrogen–carbon catalyst in a H2–O2 fuel cell at 0.5 V. DFT calculations coupled with experimental observations revealed that a zigzag carbon atom is the most active site for ORR among several types of carbon defects on graphene nanoribbons in an acid electrolyte. Further to this study, it should be interesting to selectively functionalize the zigzag edges with heteroatoms for ORR electrocatalysis and beyond.
 Fig. 11  (a–c) Schematic diagram of zigzag-type graphene nanoribbons on carbon nanotubes (GNR@CNT). (a) MWCNT, (b) partially unzipped oxidized CNT, (c) GNR@CNT. (d) The application of GNR@CNT in a PEMFC. (e and f) TEMs and schematic diagrams of nitrogen-doped GNR@CNT. Adapted with permission from ref. 144, copyright 2018 to Springer Nature. 


3.1.3. Vacancy, hole, and Stone–Wales defect for ORR. 
The second law of thermodynamics implies that a certain number of disorders or defects is unavoidable in crystalline materials,40 including carbon nanomaterials. As mentioned above (Section 2.3.2), these intrinsic structural and edge defects (pentagonal, defects at the zigzag edge, etc.) even in dopant-free nanocarbons can impart considerable ORR activities.67,144,177 Different types of defects (Fig. 12a–i),178 including single/multiple vacancies, lattice reconstructions, non-hexagonal topologies, and dangling groups,40,178–180 have been reported to promote adsorption as well as chemical and electrochemical activities.
 Fig. 12  (a) Perfect graphene cluster, (b) Stone–Wales defect (SW), (c) single vacancy (SV), (d) double vacancies (DV), (e) edge defect with pentagon ring at zigzag edge (PZ), octagon, and fused pentagon carbon rings line defect with (f) odd number of octagon rings (GLD-558-01) and (g) even number of octagon rings (GLD-558-02), and pentagon–heptagon pairs line defects with (h) odd number of heptagon rings (GLD-57-01) and (i) even number of heptagon rings (GLD-57-02). The large gray ball: carbon atom; and the small white ball: hydrogen atom, respectively. Adapted with permission from ref. 178, copyright 2015 to Royal Society of Chemistry. (j) HRTEM image of carbon nanocone (CNC700) (I: the corner, II: the broken fringe, and III: the hole), (k) The onset potential towards ORR of different defects from DFT calculations. Reproduced with permission from ref. 177, copyright 2015 to American Chemical Society. (l) Schematic structure of nitrogen-doped graphene mesh (NGM). Reproduced with permission from ref. 180, copyright 2016 to Wiley. (m) Expanded HAADF-STEM image of D-HOPG. (n) The local work functions collected from the edge areas of Ar-HOPG, N-HOPG, and D-HOPG. Inset: Scheme of the KPFM test. (o) LSV curves of Ar-HOPG (red), N-HOPG (blue), and D-HOPG (purple) towards ORR in 0.1 M H2SO4 solution). Inset: Onset potentials vs. RHE at 0.05 mA cm−2. Reproduced with permission from ref. 67, copyright 2019 to Springer Nature. 
The experimental demonstration of defect-induced electrocatalytic activities for C-MFECs was first reported in 2015 when high ORR activities were observed for defective carbon nanocages and graphene-nanoribbon-supported carbon quantum dots.164,177 Specifically, the defective carbon nanocages showed the ORR activity even better than that of the B-doped carbon nanotubes and comparable to that of the N-doped carbon nanostructures. Fig. 12j shows a typical TEM image of the carbon nanocone (CNC700), its ORR performance, and the theoretical onset potentials for different types of defects, respectively.177 The theoretical and experimental results confirmed that both zigzag edge defect and a pentagon defect were responsible for the high ORR activity in 0.1 M KOH electrolyte (Fig. 12k); both induce inhomogeneous charge distributions, as is the case with heteroatom doping.165 Subsequently, an N-doped edge-rich graphene mesh (NGM) was fabricated by direct carbonization of sticky rice as the carbon precursor (Fig. 12l), melamine as a nitrogen source, and Mg(OH)2 as a template, leading to excellent ORR (Eonset ≈ 0.89 V, E1/2 ≈ 0.77 V, Id = 6.41 mA cm−2) and OER electrocatalytic activities.180 Theoretical investigation and experimental observation again revealed that a nitrogen-free configuration with adjacent pentagon and heptagon carbon rings exhibited the lowest overpotential with 0.14 V for ORR and 0.21 V for OER.180
To confirm the defect-associated active sites more elaborately, a micro-electrocatalytic testing apparatus was recently devised to experimentally identify the active site location(s) in defective HOPG (D-HOPG, obtained by doping pyridinic N (Pr-N) at the HOPG edges and removing the N atoms through annealing at 1100 °C in N2 gas atmosphere leaving behind pentagonal defects near the lattice edges) towards the ORR.67Fig. 12m shows a typical HAADF-STEM image identifying the pentagonal defects on the D-HOPG, which possessed the lowest work function estimated by AFM studies (Fig. 12n). As determined by the macro- and micro-electrochemical measurements coupled with the work function and DFT calculations, Fig. 12o shows that the edge pentagon defect was the major active site for the acidic (0.1 M H2SO4) ORR electrocatalysis, superior to that of Pr–N in N-doped HOPG.67 Moreover, a N–S–C coordination-structured active site, originated from the integrity of edged thiophene S, graphitic N, and pentagon defects, was reconstructed from N-modified S defects in carbon aerogel with a 3D hierarchical macro–meso–micro-porous structure.165 This material exhibited an outstanding ORR activity (e.g., half-wave potentials of 0.76 V in both 0.5 M H2SO4 and 0.1 M HClO4; and 0.85 V in 0.1 M KOH) with a good stability and high current density in both acidic and alkaline electrolytes. A combined experimental and computational study revealed that the pentagon S defect is essential for the ORR in an acidic electrolyte.165 Although considerable progress on defective C-MFECs has been made,181 more fundamental studies on the defect structures (e.g., Stone–Wales defects, edges, or heteroatoms) and associated electrocatalytic properties are essential before their practical potentials could be realized.

3.2. Recent advances in C-MFECs for OER
Much like ORR is important to fuel cell technologies, OER is the heart for metal–air batteries and water splitting. Unlike ORR, however, OER generally produces a high overpotential, at which most of the transition and noble metal-based electrocatalysts are unstable. Even carbon nanomaterials used for the OER electrolytic reactions may be oxidized.8,21 So far, numerous research effort has been devoted to fabricating various efficient and stable OER electrocatalysts,21,131,182–199 including, but not limited to, vertically aligned CNTs,182 N-doped porous carbon,183 S-doped carbon,184 acid-oxidized carbon textiles (carbon cloth),185 F-doped carbon,186 graphitic carbon nitride (C3N4)/carbon nanofiber,187 3D porous C3N4/carbon micro-flowers,188 benzene derived-graphyne,189 carbon quantum dot/graphene heterostructures,190 graphene-supported N-doped carbon,191 PANi hydrogel derived N/P-codoped aerogel,71 N/S-codoped C3N4 sponges sandwiched with carbon nanocrystals,192 N/P-codoped carbon nanospheres,193 N/P-codoped covalent organic polymers,194 hard-template (SiO2) assisted N/P-codoped porous carbon,158 N/S-codoped graphitic carbon foams,195 sp-N and S codoped few-layer graphdiyne,196 phytic-acid-doped conductive hydrogels,197 P/S-codoped C3N4,198 defect-rich N-doped carbons,131,200–202 N-doped/O-functionalized/edge-defect-rich vertically aligned graphene,203 and stacked nanofilm (G–BNG) of pure graphene (G) on boron and nitrogen (BN)-codoped graphene (BNG).204 Some of them show the OER performance comparable or even superior to various commercially available noble metal-based electrocatalysts, such as Pt/C, IrO2, and/or RuO2, in terms of onset potential, overpotential, stability, current density, and/or selectivity. The materials, preparation methods, and the specific performance for most of the reported C-MECs towards OER are summarized in Table 2.
