For instance, graphene-supported N-doped carbon (G@NC) with a high degree of N doping (Fig. 13a) was synthesized by in situ self-assembling a glucaminium-based ionic liquid on the pristine graphene under hydrothermal condition.191Fig. 13b and c shows the electrocatalytic OER performance of the G@NC along with its reaction energy for OER. This carbon based nanohybrid with a 2D structure showed a current density of 10 mA cm−2 at an overpotential of 330 mV and a Tafel slope of 52.6 mV dec−1, both of which are among the lowest reported values for metal-free OER catalysts and are even lower than those of the Ir- and Ru-based catalysts.191 An good stability of G@NC was found after 10 h of chronopotentiometric testing at 10 mA cm−2 as the current density at 2.0 V decreased by only 6.9% after 800 cycles of cyclic voltammetry. The observed outstanding electrocatalytic performance was attributed to the high graphitic-N content assembled on graphene, which greatly increased CACs for the OER, and the pristine graphene support acted as cocatalyst significantly promoting the OER activity of the C sites adjacent to the graphitic N atoms (Fig. 13c).191
 Fig. 13  (a) Schematic diagram of the structure of GIL. (b) LSV curves of different GIL samples and the commercial RuO2. (c) Reaction energy for each step of the OER process from calculation. Adapted from ref. 191, copyright 2019 to Wiley-VCH. (d) The volcano plots of OER versus the difference between the binding energies of O and OH (ΔGO* − ΔGOH*) for the various reactive clusters. (e and f) The atomic structures of the N2CP1C–O and N2CP12C–O. Orange ball: P atom, blue ball: N atom, and grey ball: C atom. (g) Free energy diagrams along the ORR and OER pathways for the NxCP1C series clusters. (h) Free energy differences based on the diagrams in (g) for ORR-RDS1 and ORR-RDS2, and OER-RDS. Adapted from ref. 208, copyright 2019 to AIP Publishing. 
As mentioned above, N/P-codoping has also attracted considerable recent attention for efficient OER electrocatalysis.71,158,193,194,205,209 Theoretical studies have revealed that N and P atoms prefer to bond with each other, forming embedded N–P clusters in graphene. As shown in Fig. 13d–h, the N–P complex defects (NxCP1C and NxCP12C) are energetically more favored than individual NC and PC defects, suggesting that heterogeneous clusters might have been embedded in graphene.208 The ORR and OER performance of the N–P clusters can be finely tuned over a wide range by changing the N:P ratio. Fig. 13h shows that the OER catalytic activity can be optimized when the N:P ratio reaches ∼2 while the catalytic performance for ORR would be maximized at the N:P ratio of ∼3. Thus, the NxCP1C clusters in the graphene sheet show great potential in achieving efficient OER/ORR bi-functional electrocatalysis.208 In a good agreement with recent experimental observations, these results shed light on the microscopic understanding of the active sites in the N/P-codoped graphene for ORR and OER, providing insight into the controlled growth of effective bifunctional oxygen electrocatalysts.
Recently, certain C-MFECs have also been demonstrated to show OER property via intermolecular charge transfer. Fig. 14a–c shows the high OER performance and good stability for the C60/SWCNTs in an O2-saturated 0.5 M H2SO4 solution, whereas the OER performance of the pure C60 and SWCNTs were relatively low.46 Exposure of C60/SWCNTs to visible light (mercury lamp, 1 W cm−2) enhanced the ORR/HER/OER electrocatalytic activities in either an O2-saturated KOH solution or in a phosphate buffer solution. Another example can be found for a stacked nanofilm (G–BNG) of pure graphene (G) on boron and nitrogen (BN)-codoped graphene (BNG) with a total thickness of about 2–3 nm,204 the B and N dopants in the bottom layer influence the π-bonding environment in the top G layer (Fig. 14d), facilitating the effective electron/charge transfer for OER electrocatalysis, making the G–BNG film promising for advanced renewable energy conversion systems. G–BNG showed a superior OER performance and stability (lowest Tafel slope of 143.22 mV dec−1 and a small overpotential of 0.58 V along with 81.6% retention of the current density at 1.8 V vs. RHE after 50000 s of operation) to that of a stacked G–G layer, G–BG film, or G–NG film in 0.1 M KOH medium. Good OER performance with a small overpotential of 265 mV at 10 mA cm−2, a Tafel slope of 89 mV dec−1, and long term stability of a 12 h for sustained operation at 10 mA cm−2 with a negligible current loss of <4% was also obtained for a GH-BGQD hybrid, in which B-doped graphene quantum dots (B-GQDs) were anchored onto the porous graphene hydrogel (GH) of a large surface area via π–π stacking to induce the intermolecular charge-transfer.206 In another study, a modified dip-coat-dry method was developed for fabricating a carbon cloth (PA–PPy/CC) coated with a conductive and porous hydrogel layer by simply dipping the carbon cloth into a polypyrrole (Py) solution with phytic acid (PA) and (NH4)2S2O8.197 As a result, the PA–PPy/CC delivered excellent OER performance with a low overpotential of 340 mV at 10 mA cm−2, Tafel slope of 54.9 mV dec−1, and 20 h long-term stability due to the fast charge transfer associated with the conductive PPy network and the efficient mass transport through the vesicular nanostructure that also has a large surface area for a maximum exposure of the active sites.
 Fig. 14  (a and b) Polarization curves and the corresponding Tafel plots of pure C60, SWCNTs, C60–SWCNT15, and RuO2 towards OER in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte. (c) I–t curves at 1.7 V versus RHE for C60–SWCNT15, and RuO2 catalysts. Inset: LSV of OER curves for C60–SWCNT15 before and after 1.5 h I–t test. Reproduced with permission from ref. 46, copyright 2019 to American Chemical Society. (d) Electrocatalytic OER mechanism t in a G/BNG stacked nanofilm. Reproduced with permission from ref. 204, copyright 2020 to American Chemical Society. (e) Schematic synthetic process of EBP@NG. Reproduced with permission from ref. 207. Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH. 
In addition, a metal-free 2D/2D heterostructure hybrid, composing of exfoliated black phosphorus (EBP) and N-doped graphene (NG), was demonstrated to show efficient charge transfer for efficient OER (Fig. 14e) with a lower overpotential of 310 mV at 10 mA cm−2, better than that of the individual EBP (>500 mV at 10 mA cm−2) and NG (430 mV at 10 mA cm−2) and even comparable to that of the commercial RuO2 (300 mV at 10 mA cm−2).207 Moreover, the resultant EBP@NG remained 95.7% current density after a stability test for 10 hours, which was superior to the commercial Ir/C. The enhanced electrochemical stability was attributed to the protection of the NG by the ultrathin EBP, and the enhanced OER activity was arising from the abundant activated carbon sites on the positively charged NG induced by charge-transfer with the EBP. Thus, excellent OER performance can be achieved by rational design and development of C-MFECs with desirable structures to facilitate the charge transfer.
3.3. Recent advances in C-MFECs for HER
The electrocatalytic HER (2H+ + 2e− → H2) provides an efficient and clean pathway for the mass production of hydrogen fuel from water splitting and requires highly active electrocatalysts.210–212 Although photoreduction of water to hydrogen catalysed by poly(p-phenylene) was reported in 1985,213 the first C-MFEC based on g-C3N4 coupled with N-doped graphene (Fig. 15a) was introduced in 2014 to replace the costly Pt-based catalysts for electrocatalytic H2 evolution.13 As seen from energy diagrams of the pure g-C3N4 and g-C3N4@NG in Fig. 15a–d from DFT calculations, HER properties of the g-C3N4@NG are originated from an intrinsic chemical and electronic coupling that synergistically promotes the proton adsorption and reduction kinetics.13 Furthermore, HER results shown in Fig. 15e and f indicates that g-C3N4 is a potential metal-free electrocatalyst for efficient HER in acidic electrolytes even compared with various metal-based HER electrocatalysts. Indeed, the g-C3N4@NG outperformed many transition metal-based electrocatalysts for the HER.214,215
 Fig. 15  (a) Interfacial electron transfer in C3N4@NG. (b) Band structure of pure g-C3N4 (blue curves) and C3N4@NG hybrid (red curves). (c) The DOS on pure g-C3N4 and C3N4@NG hybrid. (d) The free-energy diagram of HER at the equilibrium potential for metal-free catalysts and Pt. (e) Volcano plots of i0 as a function of the DGH* for C3N4@NG, metal catalysts as well as a MoS2 catalyst. (f) Free-energy diagram of HER on the surface of C3N4@NG under H* coverage conditions of 1/3, 2/3, and 1. Reproduced with permission from ref. 13, copyright 2014 to Springer Nature. 
As shown in Fig. 16a–c, the graphitic planes in g-C3N4 are constructed from tri-s-triazine units connected by planar amino groups, providing abundant edges and active sites with low conductivity.216–221 In order to reduce the high intrinsic electrical resistance in g-C3N4, heteroatom(s) functionalization and hybridization with highly conducting carbon nanomaterials/polymers have been produced for enhancing the electrocatalytic and photocatalytic activities of g-C3N4.214–226 Examples include, but not limited to, carbon doped g-C3N4 (C-rich g-C3N4),214 C3N4 quantum dot and graphene hybrids (CNQDs@G),215 C3N4-based polymers,216 N-doped g-C3N4,219 S-doped g-C3N4,222 S/Se codoped g-C3N4,225 and polymeric C3N4.220,221,226
 Fig. 16  (a) Schematic diagram of a g-C3N4 sheet. Reproduced with permission from ref. 217, copyright 2019 to Springer Nature. (b and c) Top and side view of pristine g-C3N4 with numbered N atoms from 1 to 8 and numbered C atoms from 9 to 14. (d) B-CN, (e) P-CN, and (f) SCN (B and P atoms replace the C13, and S atom substitutes the N7). Reproduced with permission from ref. 226, copyright 2017 to American Chemical Society. (g) Volcano-shaped relationship between overpotential and descriptor for the most stable doped (D) g-C3N4 (onset potential [CN/PC] vs. the descriptor (ϕ)). (h) The predicted overpotentials were plotted as a function of the experimental onset potential of D-g-C3N4. Adapted with permission from ref. 227, copyright 2019 to http://www.frontiersin.org. 
Along with the materials synthesis, comprehensive experimental and theoretical studies have also been performed to examine the effects of dopant components on the catalytic performance of g-C3N4-based C-MFECs. For this purpose, g-C3N4 monoliths with three types of dopant elements (B, P, and S) embedded in different sites (either C or N) of the C–N skeleton were rationally designed and synthesized (Fig. 16d–f).226 It was revealed that S atom acted as the best dopant while B and P elements deteriorated the HER activity for g-C3N4 monoliths. For the S-doped g-C3N4, a low onset potential of only −55 mV was required to efficiently implement the HER, and an operational current density of 10 mA cm−2 was achieved at −186 mV.226 Furthermore, the S-doped g-C3N4 exhibited the lowest charge-transfer resistance with the most favorable overall HER kinetics resulted from both the high intrinsic activity of individual CACs and abundant exposed catalytic sites.
Recent theoretical investigation has revealed the favorable configurations for C3N4 based materials towards HER. Generally speaking, a favored HER catalyst should feature a |ΔGH*| value around zero to allow for optimal adsorption/desorption kinetics.226 Compared with the pristine g-C3N4 (−0.19 eV), the B- and P-doped composites featured more negative ΔGH* values (−2.25 and −1.87 eV, respectively), indicating a more difficult desorption process of the H* species. In contrast, the S-embedded hybrid catalyst exhibited a desired ΔGH* value of −0.03 eV, which is even close to that of the well-known Pt-based catalyst (|ΔGH*| = 0.09 eV). As such, S-doped g-C3N4 acted as an n-type semiconductor, and the S-atoms introduced an impurity band near the conducting band minimum (CBM), and the electrons could be excited into the CBM even by thermal excitation, favorable for H+ to capture electrons.226 Therefore, the dopant components within the g-C3N4 had distinct and substantial effects on the overall electrocatalytic activities.
In another study, g-C3N4 was hybridized/coupled with nanoporous graphene codoped with S and Se (g-C3N4@S–Se-pGr) to synergistically promote the proton adsorption and reduction kinetics and to show attractive electrocatalytic performance, including an exchange current density of 6.27 × 10−6 A cm−2, an on-set potential of 0.092 V, a Tafel slope of 86 mV dec−1, and an adsorption free energy of −0.13 eV with a long-term cycling stability comparable to those of commercial Pt/C catalysts in both acidic and alkaline electrolytes.226 Furthermore, the theoretical volcano relationship between the overpotential and the descriptor ϕ was established, from which the most active dopants could be predicted, as shown in Fig. 16g and h. The S, Se, and O sitting at the summit of the volcano are expected to be the most active heteroatom dopants for g-C3N4.227 On the surface of the heteroatom(s) doped g-C3N4, the hybridized energy levels of the two atoms split into two groups: the bonding orbital and anti-bonding states with the antibonding state being essential for absorption by accepting electrons. Also, it was concluded that doping near the g-C3N4 edge is promising for developing highly active g-C3N4-based C-MFECs for HER.227
Very recently, a 2D/2D van der Waals heterostructure of phosphorene/g-C3N4 was fabricated to show an enhanced visible-light photocatalytic H2 production activity of 571 μmol h−1 g−1 in 18 v% lactic acid aqueous solution due to the intimate electronic coupling at the 2D/2D heterojunction interface.228 Owing to its p-type semiconducting characteristics, Graphdiyne has also been used as a hole transport layer in photoelectrochemical H2 production from water.229 The GDY based photocathode evolved H2 with 90 ± 5% faradaic efficiency for stable operation over 12 hours.229
Apart from C3N4-based metal-free catalysts, a series of non-metal heteroatom(s) (e.g., N, P, S, B, F) doped/codoped carbon materials have also been studied both experimentally and theoretically for HER.220–240 Examples include, but not limited to, N-doped CNTs,230 N-carbon nanosheets,231 N-doped graphene microtubes,232 N-doped carbon framework and CNT hybrids,233 N-doped amine (–NH2) functionalized graphene,234 N-doped graphene quantum dot decorated graphite foam,235 N-doped plasma etched 3D graphene,236 N-doped porous carbon,237 B-doped graphene,238 Ni-foam template-synthesized S-doped 3D graphene foam,239 B/N-codoped carbon,240 CNTs modified with various functional groups,241 N/P-codoped nanoporous graphene,242 N/P-codoped carbon network,243 N/P-codoped carbon nanofiber network,244 N/P-codoped holey graphene,245 N/P-codoped porous carbon derived from bacterium,246 N/S-codoped carbon tubes,247 N/S-codoped nanoporous graphene,248 N/S-codoped porous graphene sheets,38 and N/S-codoped porous carbon fabricated from human hair.249 Besides, heteroatom-free carbon materials, such as graphene sponge,250 graphene networks with abundant sharp edges,251 and C-MFECs based on black phosphorus or oxidized black phosphorus nanosheets, have also been demonstrated to show promising HER performance.252,253 The materials, preparation methods, and the specific performance of the reported C-MECs for the HER are summarized in Table 3.

Along with heteroatom(s) doping discussed above, the introduction of edges, vacancies, and/or holes into C-MFECs has also been exploited to effectively enhance the electrocatalytic HER performance. For instance, a 3D N/P-codoped graphene electrode (3DNG-P, Fig. 17a) with a freestanding 3D porous architecture, high-level N-doping, and plasma-induced enriched defects was developed as a highly active C-MFEC for HER to show a low overpotential of 128 mV at 10 mA cm−2, small Tafel slope of 66 mV dec−1, and large exchange current density of 0.11 mA cm−2 in an acidic electrolyte (0.5 M H2SO4, Fig. 17b).236 A favorable HER activity (Fig. 17c) and stability over a wide pH range (0.5 M H2SO4, 1.0 M PBS, and 1.0 M KOH) were observed.
 Fig. 17  (a) Synthesis procedures of 3DNG-P. (b) LSV curves of HER for 3DG, 3DNG, 3DNG-P, and commercial 20% Pt/C catalysts in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte, (c) Polarization curves of 3DNG-P in medias including 0.5 M H2SO4, 1.0 M PBS and 1 M KOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 236, copyright 2017 to Elsevier B.V. (d) The preparation process of 3D graphene networks with abundant sharp edges. (e) The comparison of HER activity of 3D graphene networks and its counterparts in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte. (f) Electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution at the edge site. Reproduced with permission from ref. 251 copyright 2018 to Wiley-VCH. (g) TEM image of N-GQDs/GF and (h) polarization curves of GF, N-GQDs, N-GQDs/GF, and Pt electrodes in 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte. Reproduced with permission from ref. 235, copyright 2019 to Elsevier Ltd. 
Also, a one-step CVD strategy was developed for the controllable growth of a 3D graphene network consisting of a large number of sharp edge sites (Fig. 17d).251 The resultant metal-free and dopant-free 3D graphene network showed highly efficient electrocatalytic HER performance with an extremely low onset potential of ca. 18 mV in 0.5 M H2SO4 solution and good stability, which is much lower than that of certain heteroatom-doped C-MFECs and even comparable to those of transition-metal-hybrids.251 As can be seen in Fig. 17e, the 3D graphene network with sharp edges showed a much better HER performance than these of vertical graphene sheets on a planar Si, 2D graphene, Si substrate, SiOx nanowire network, and even close to that of the 20% Pt/C in 0.5 M H2SO4. The combined experimental and DFT analyses revealed that the excellent HER performance observed for the 3D graphene network was attributed to the presence of abundant edge sites, good conductivity, and seamless contact between the graphene sheets and the substrate (Fig. 17f). Combining with in-depth theoretical investigations, therefore, the strategic designing of C-MFECs for providing high conducting properties and CASs are essential for realizing advanced HER electrocatalysis comparable to those of noble metal-based precious catalysts. Similarly, N-doped graphene quantum dots (N-GQDs, ∼5 nm) were attached to a graphite foam (GF) by a facile hydrothermal route,235 and the obtained N-GQDs/GF composite acted as an ultra-active free-standing electrode for electrochemical HER and activation of peroxymonosulfate to degrade phenol pollutants, showing an overpotential of only 72 mV vs. RHE at 10 mA cm−2 for electrochemical HER with a Tafel slope of 84 mV dec−1 and an onset potential similar to that of Pt catalyst.235Fig. 17g and h show TEM image and HER performance of the N-GQDs/GF electrode.235 Despite the considerable progress that has been achieved with C-MFECs for HER, a deeper mechanistic understanding is needed for further improvement of their electrocatalytic performance.254
Although great progress has been made, the development of effective C-MFCs for acidic electrolytes is still challenging. The ORR reaction pathways and mechanisms in acidic and alkaline electrolytes are different,255 and the Gibbs free energy on the C-MFECs (defective graphene) towards ORR in acidic mediums obtained through a computational approach is higher than that in alkaline conditions.256 Some researchers speculated the stability of certain N functional groups on the surface of C-MFECs is relatively weak in the acidic medium257 due to an insufficient number of ORR “active sites” on the C-MFECs in acid medium.258,259 However, it is believed that the catalytic performance could be largely improved by combining co/multi-doping with other effects (dopant type, dopant location, defective, and electrode structure). Indeed, high ORR activities in acid electrolyte were reported for new active sites of edged pentagon defects and with N, S dopants.165
To improve the activities of C-MFECs towards HER, the most commonly used method is to tune the electronic structure of C-MFECs by N doping.260,261 However, both experiments and DFT calculations revealed that single-N dopant in a six-membered ring of the carbon skeleton cannot tune the electronic structure of C-MFECs to obtain a favorable adsorption energy for H*.260 Recent combined experimental and DFT studies indicate that the most promising C-MFECs to achieve the performance of precious metal catalysts are 3D graphene network with sharp edge sites251 and porous carbon with dual graphitic N dopants in one six-membered carbon rings.261 The carbon atom bonded with two adjacent graphitic N atoms is beneficial for activating C–H bonds, thereby leading to a low Gibbs free energy for H* adsorption (ΔGH*) for the significantly improved HER performance, overwhelmed those of most transition-metal-based and carbon-based catalysts, and even approached the benchmarks of precious metals.261 Therefore, controlling the morphology of carbon materials and the location of heteroatoms shows great promise for improving the catalytic HER activity of C-MFECs.
For the OER catalytic activity on C-MFECs, the common problem is the oxidation of the carbon materials under the potential during catalyzing processes.8,21 Nevertheless, the long-term stability of the C-MFECs is highly dependent on the quality of the carbon materials (e.g., graphitization degree, nature of the dopants and/or defects). Very stable OER electrocatalysts with a long-term stability and durability in whole pH region have been demonstrated for C-MFECs of high quality. Even under harsh conditions for OER in the water-splitting process, the high catalytic activity of C-MFECs can significantly reduce the overpotential to further stabilize the carbon electrodes. For instance, the nanohybrid of N-doped carbon supported on graphene with a high content of N doping showed small values of overpotential and Tafel slop, both of which are among the lowest reported values for OER C-MFECs and even those of the precious metals Ir-/Ru-based catalysts.188
3.4. C-MFECs for electrochemical transformation
As mentioned above, renewable energy technologies, such as batteries, fuel cells, and water-splitting, can help solve current energy and environmental challenges. The aforementioned ORR, OER, and HER dominate the energy conversion and storage processes in these renewable energy technologies. These electrocatalytic reactions provide a facile and scalable solution to store energy in and retrieve energy from chemical bonds through the interconversion between chemical and electrical energy. In this regard, C-MFECs have recently been demonstrated to act as cost-effective, eco-friendly, and efficient photo-/electro-catalysts for numerous chemical transformations under ambient temperature and pressure, including two-electron transfer ORR to produce H2O2,7 NRR for the synthesis of ammonia at ambient conditions, CO2RR for direct conversion of CO2 into various hydrocarbon-based fuels and value-added chemicals, along with CO2 evolution from carbon for energy applications, and photo-/electro-chemical generation of chemicals powered by renewable energy (solar, wind, etc.).4,8,262–264

3.4.1. C-MFECs for nitrogen reduction (NRR). 
Ammonia (NH3), as a key precursor for fertilizer production, efficient hydrogen carrier as a clean fuel, and one of the essential industrial chemicals for both human beings and the earth's ecosystem, plays an indispensable role in the agricultural, plastic, and pharmaceutical and textile industries.265–268 Having twice volumetric energy density as compared to liquid hydrogen, NH3 is an ideal hydrogen carrier for efficient energy storage. There are three typical pathways to synthesize NH3: azotobacter nitrogen fixation (in nature by nitrogenase enzymes), Haber–Bosch process (industrial production), and electrocatalysis. The conventional Haber–Bosch process for NH3 production involves the reaction of H2 and N2 at high temperatures (350–550 °C) and under high pressure (150–350 atm). Among these pathways, the electrocatalytic production of NH3 through NRR is very promising as it is energy efficient and can be cost-effectively performed at ambient conditions.6,8,266 Traditionally, noble/transition metal-based catalysts are required to promote the electrocatalytic NRR. The high cost of the precious metal catalysts, coupled with their limited reserve, however, has precluded the electrochemical NRR from large-scale commercial applications. Furthermore, metal-based NRR electrocatalysts often suffer from multiple drawbacks, including (i) the adsorption energy of N2 molecules with most of the transition metals is too weak for activation towards NRR, and (ii) the d orbital electrons in most of the transition metals generally trigger the formation of metal–H bonds for initiating undesired HER, leading to a low faradaic efficiency (FE) for NRR. Thus, the NRR performance of transition metal catalysts need to be further improved, though several transition metals can paralyze the HER at appropriate potentials.8,269,270
Since 2018, certain C-MFECs have been demonstrated to show great potential as NRR electrocatalysts. For the high catalytic performance and selectivity, however, electrocatalysts must be rationally designed to optimize the mass transport, chemi(physi)sorption, and proton/electron transfer. For NRR, the main side reaction is HER, in which most protons or H2O in the system are reduced to H2 rather than NH3, leading to a poor selectivity.8,266 Extensive investigations have been conducted to improve the NRR performance of C-MFECs.8,271Fig. 18a schematically shows the NRR process,271 which involves two major fundamental mechanisms, namely the dissociative and associative pathways with different intermediates, along with an alternative associative distal pathway (Fig. 18b–d).265,271
 Fig. 18  Schematic illustrations of (a) electrocatalysis of NRR and its possible mechanisms, including (b) dissociative pathway, (c) associative distal pathway, and (d) associative alternating pathway. Adapted from ref. 271, copyright 2019 to Elsevier Inc. 
So far, N-doped porous carbon derived from MOFs,17,272 B-doped graphene,273 defect rich polymeric C3N4,274 B-doped BN edges,275 defect abundant carbon cloth,276 black P nanosheet decorated g-C3N4,277 S-dots/graphene nanohybrid,278 O-doped graphdiyne,279 N-doped heterocyclic carbine,280 P-doped C3N4 with surface N vacancies,281 N/P-codoped multilayer graphene,282 N/P-codoped porous carbon,283 polyaniline,284 and N-doped carbon nanospikes have been utilized for efficient electrocatalytic NRR in both alkaline and acidic electrolytes.285 In particular, a highly textured C-MFEC, consisting of N-doped carbon nanospikes (CNS, Fig. 19a), was developed for electrochemically reducing dissolved N2 gas to ammonia in an aqueous electrolyte under ambient conditions, showing a FE of 11.56 ± 0.85% at −1.19 V vs. RHE and maximum production rate of 97.18 ± 7.13 μg h−1 cm−2 (Fig. 19b and c).285 This catalyst has a surface composed of sharp spikes, which concentrates the electric field at the tips to promote the electroreduction of dissolved N2 molecules near the electrode.
 Fig. 19  (a) The TEM image of CNS with folded graphene layers due to the presence of N heteroatoms. (b) Ammonia generation on the CNS electrode compared to the O-etched CNS and glassy carbon controls in 0.25 M LiClO4 electrolyte. (c) FEs reflect the formation rates, with the highest efficiency of 11.56 ± 0.85% at −1.19 V vs. RHE. Reproduced with permission from ref. 285, copyright 2018 to American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS). (d) Structural details of BG for NRR in 0.05 M H2SO4 electrolyte along with schematic for the electron density and the atomic orbital of BC3 for binding N2. (e) NRR stability test of BG at −0.5 V vs. RHE in 0.05 M H2SO4 electrolyte. Reproduced with permission from ref. 273, copyright 2018 to Elsevier Inc. (f) The NH3 yields and FEs at −0.85 V during the recycling test 5 times using S-dots graphene nanohybrid (SDG) in 0.5 M LiClO4 electrolyte. Reproduced with permission from ref. 278, copyright 2019 to Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Furthermore, B-doped graphene (BG) with different boron structures were rationally designed and synthesized to enhance the adsorption of N2 in an aqueous solution (0.05 M H2SO4) at ambient conditions (Fig. 19d).273 With a doping level of 6.2%, BG achieved an NH3 production rate of 9.8 μg h−1 cm−2 and an relatively good FE of 10.8% at −0.5 V vs. RHE, as shown in Fig. 19e. The observed remarkable NRR activity is attributed to the doping-induced charge polarization to positively charge the B atoms due to the larger electronegativity of carbon (2.55) than that of boron (2.04). DFT calculations revealed the reaction pathway in the acidic electrolyte to be N2 → N2* → NNH → N* + NH3(g) → NH* → NH2* → NH3* → NH3(g).8,273 Furthermore, the lower reaction energy barrier for the rate-determining step (N* → NH*) on BC3 (0.43 eV) than that on BCO2 (1.04 eV) and BC2O (1.30 eV) makes BC3 (see, Fig. 19d for the structure and electron distribution) most favorable among these B-doped carbon materials for electrocatalytic NRR.
In another related but independent study, S-dots and graphene nanohybrid (SDG) was fabricated and utilized as a metal-free electrocatalyst for ambient N2-to-NH3 conversion with an excellent selectivity.278 This electrocatalyst, when operated in 0.5 M LiClO4, exhibited a high NH3 yield of 28.56 μg h−1 mgcat−1 and a FE of 7.07% at −0.85 V vs. RHE, along with excellent electrochemical stability (Fig. 19f).278 N-doped C-MFECs have also been adapted for efficient NRR, DFT calculations revealed the preferable pathway for ammonia synthesis (*NN → *NHNH → *NH2NH2 → 2NH3) in an alkaline medium (e.g., 0.1 M KOH) and found that the NRR on pyridinic (0.45 eV) and pyrrolic N (0.56 eV) sites were energetically more favorable than the graphitic N sites.8,272
Certain defect engineering strategies have also been demonstrated to be efficient for imparting high NRR performance to the metal-free polymeric carbon nitride (PCN) catalyst. In particular, nitrogen vacancy (NV) defects were controllably introduced onto PCN to produce tunable NVs contained PCN (denoted as: PCN-NVx, x represents the re-calcination hours in Ar). For PCN-NVx, the NH3 yield was 8.09 μg h−1 mgcat−1, and the FE is 11.59% (Fig. 20a and b).274 As shown in Fig. 20c–f, DFT calculations revealed that dinitrogen molecules could be chemisorbed on as the engineered nitrogen vacancies of PCN through constructing a dinuclear end-on bound structure for spatial electron transfer from adjacent C atoms.274 Furthermore, the NN bond length of adsorbed N2 increases dramatically, facilitating the activation process for electrocatalytic reduction of N2 into NH3.
 Fig. 20  (a) Yield of NH3 on PCN and PCN-NV4 at different given potentials. (b) Ammonia yield on different catalysts at −0.2 V vs. RHE. (c) N2 adsorption on PCN with “N” vacancy. (d and e) The charge density difference on the N2-adsorbed PCN with “N” vacancy. Yellow isosurface: charge accumulation; blue isosurface: charge depletion. (f) Free energy diagram towards NRR on PCN with “N” vacancies at equilibrium potential. Reproduced with permission from ref. 274, copyright 2018 to Wiley-VCH. 
The NRR reaction selectivity can also be effectively improved by using an electrolyte with a limited proton transfer rate and increased solubility of N2. Thus, the NRR electrolyte selection is also important. Two main types of electrolytes, namely the liquid and solid states, have been used for electrocatalytic NRR. A strong correlation between the solubility of N2 in the electrolytes and the NH3 production rate has been observed for various liquid electrolytes, including aqueous KOH, aqueous HCl, aqueous Li2SO4, and mixed alcohol/water.271 On the other hand, solid-state electrolytes, especially Nafion membranes as the solid H+ conductors, were demonstrated to be promising for enhancing the mass transfer of N2 toward the catalyst surface and inhibiting the occurrence of the HER, thereby effectively overcoming the low conversion and FE for NRR.266–268 To achieve high-performance NRR for C-MFECs. Therefore, appropriate electrolytes should be used, and the electrolytic NRR cell should be carefully constructed.269,270


3.4.2. C-MFECs for carbon dioxide conversion. 
The overuse of fossil fuels has caused excessive CO2 emissions, leading to severe environmental pollution and global warming, along with the associated climate change and sea-level rise.286–288 Therefore, it is important to reduce the increasing atmospheric CO2 concentration by, for example, directly convert CO2 into fuels and/or commodity chemicals. In this context, CO2RR provides an attractive option to convert waste carbon emissions into valuable products. In fact, numerous chemicals can be produced from CO2, including alcohol, oxygenates, and synthesis gas (syngas). The use of renewable energy sources (e.g., solar, wind, and hydroelectric) to power electrochemical reduction of CO2 to value-added chemicals could also address the non-dispatchable issue of renewable energy by providing energy storage in chemical bonds.262 As such, CO2 fixation is recognized to be a much-needed component of a carbon-neutral energy strategy. Indeed, sunlight-driven conversion of CO2 to fuels is a particularly attractive means to store intermittent solar energy in the form of C–C and C–H bonds within C1–C3 hydrocarbons.289–292
There are various methods for CO2 conversion and utilization, including thermocatalytic, photocatalytic, electrocatalytic CO2 reduction using homogeneous as well as heterogeneous catalysts.286,287,293,294 Although the thermocatalytic hydrogenation of CO2 into commodity chemicals or fuels aligns well with the current industrial reactor infrastructures, this energy-intensive process involves an elevated operation temperature (>200 °C) and a high pressure of ∼1 MPa with a low CO2 conversion efficiency.287 Furthermore, this process requires excessive H2 supply beyond the product stoichiometry as H2 is used as a reducing agent.294–296
Compared to the harsh thermochemical hydrogenation, the electrochemical conversion of the CO2 process is operated at ambient conditions and easy to scale up.286,287,293,297–302 Furthermore, electricity generated from renewable sources, such as solar, tidal, wind, and geothermal energy with a foreseen low price, can enable an energy transition from fossil fuel to a renewable energy powered electrocatalysis.286,287 Besides, water can be used as the proton source for CO2RR so that the external H2 supply is disregarded, more importantly, the CO2RR can enable to store intermittent renewable and off-peak electricity into chemicals and fuels, such as carbon monoxide (CO), methane (CH4), formate (–COOH), methanol (CH3OH), ethylene (C2H4), ethanol (C2H5OH), and propanol (C3H7OH) with a high energy density and a big market potential, as shown in Fig. 21.302
 Fig. 21  (a) Market size (Mt) and market price ($ per t) of the products from CO2RR. (b) Energy density and energy values of the CO2RR products (kW h kg−1). (c) Comparison between market price and levelized production cost for the main products from CO2RR of CO, formate, ethanol, and ethylene. (d–g) Dependence of levelized cost for CO, formate, ethylene, and ethanol on the electricity price (cents kW h−1), cell voltage (V), faradaic efficiency (%), current density (mA cm−2), capital cost (electrolyzer, $ per m), and CO2 cost ($ per t). Reproduced with permission and details from ref. 302, copyright 2019 to Wiley VCH. 
As shown in Fig. 22a–c, the electrochemical CO2 splitting and conversion are generally carried out in single-, two-, and three-compartment electrolyzers.299 Single compartment cell is free from ohmic loss while the two-compartment set-up keeps the two electrodes to support separated reactions, which is suitable to examine the mechanism for CO2RR in a low CO2 environment.303 The three-compartment electrolyzer with flow gas and electrolyte (flow cell) is introduced to achieve a high production rate for CO2 electrolysis (Fig. 22d).303 However, the linear molecule CO2 is stable and chemically inert with a low electron affinity. As such, CO2 transformation is dominated by nucleophilic attacks at the carbon atom, which is an uphill process requiring a substantial input of energy (∼750 kJ mol−1 for the dissociation of the “CO” bond).286,287,293,297–300 Furthermore, CO2RR often occurs in an aqueous electrolyte, in which CO2 dissolves very poorly at acidic or neutral pH values. Therefore, various homogeneous and/or heterogeneous catalysts have been used to accelerate this kinetically slow reduction reaction. So far, major efforts have been made to develop heterogeneous electrocatalysts, including (bi-)metals, metal oxides and sulfides, organic frameworks, and C-MFECs, for CO2RR.286,287,293,297–302
 Fig. 22  Scheme of CO2 splitting in (a) a single-compartment electrolyzer; (b) a electrolyzer with two-compartment using a bipolar membrane to separate basic and neutral electrolytes; (c) a two-compartment electrolyzer with a proton/anion exchange membrane separating the catholyte and anolyte. (d) A flow cell electrolyzer with three-compartment and a membrane separating the electrolyte(s); and adapted with permission from ref. 303, copyright 2019 to Wiley-VCH. (e) Bioelectrocatalytic pathways toward long-chain chemicals: syngas could be synthesized from CO2RR with a high selectivity by Ag- or Au-based catalysts (top left). Alternatively, CO2 can be converted into hydrocarbon and oxygenate chemicals using Cu-, Sn-, or Pd-based catalysts (bottom left), which can then be used as inputs for genetically engineered enzymes and bacteria to convert to more complex products. Adapted with permission from ref. 262, copyright 2019 to American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS). 
Unlike metal-based catalysts, the carbon-based nanostructures naturally break the scaling relationship between the adsorption of reaction intermediates and related activation energies,287 thereby, C-MFECs are promising to reduce the overpotential for efficient CO2RR. Indeed, heteroatom-doped C-MFECs in various architectures with sp2 and/or sp3 hybridization have exhibited good performance for CO2RR, yielding C2 and CO products with a FE up to and even above 90% at a decent overpotential comparable to that of Cu and Ag-/Au-based catalysts, respectively.4,287 Besides, the heteroatom-doped C-MFECs with covalent chemical bonds between the carbon base and dopant atoms should have no segregation issue, leading to a good stability, whereas metal nanoparticles often suffer segregation problems during CO2RR. Therefore, C-MFECs are promising catalysts for CO2RR.
In 2013, N-doped carbon nanofiber (N-CNF) was used as the first C-MFEC for CO2RR to carbon monoxide (CO).16 The N-CNF demonstrated a current density 13 times higher towards CO2RR compared to the bulk Ag catalyst, demonstrating the promise for C-MFECs to replace costly metal-based electrocatalysts.16 Subsequently, considerable efforts have been devoted to CO2RR by C-MFECs to consistently show comparable or even superior efficiency to metal electrodes.4,8 In this context, N-CNF, N or B doped diamonds as well as B/N-codoped diamond have been examined for efficient CO2RR.304 There are many sequential reaction pathways for converting CO2 to chemicals/fuels, such as single- (C1) or multi-carbon (C2+) oxygenates and hydrocarbons. Leveraging these reaction sequences, one approach is to first convert CO2 into stable intermediate species that can be further upgraded to the desired product(s) using biocatalysts (e.g., enzymes, bacteria), as shown in Fig. 22e.262–265,305
Since the report on the first C-MFEC (i.e., N-CNF) for CO2RR in 2013,16 numerous N-doped C-MFECs having different structures have been developed to show efficient CO2RR activities comparable or superior to those of metal-based electrocatalysts, leading to various value-added chemical products. Examples include, but not limited to, N-doped nanocarbon,306 N-CNT and ultrathin carbon nanosheet hybrid,307 N-doped carbon wrapped CNT (CN-CNTs),308 N-rich C3N4 nanotubes,309 MOF derived N-doped porous carbon,310 N-doped CNTs,311,312 N-doped CNT penuts,313 N-doped porous carbon derived from coal,314 N-doped graphene foam,315 N-doped diamond,316 N/F-codoped holey carbon sheets,317 N/P-codoped fullerene-like carbon,318 N-doped graphene and CNT hybrid,319 N/S-codoped porous carbon nanofiber,320 S-doped and S/N-codoped polymer derived carbons,321 F-doped carbon,322 B/F/N-tridoped carbon,323 defect-rich graphene and carbon,324,325 graphitic and polymeric C3N4,326,327 C3N4/CNT composite,328,329 and 3D C3N4 based materials.330 Nevertheless, the role of different heteroatom species (such as graphitic N, pyridinic N, pyrrolic N) played306,308 and the effects of topological defects and edge defects (zigzag and armchair edges) on CO2RR require further studies.
To optimize the electrocatalytic CO2RR performance, Cui et al., developed a steam etching strategy to tune the nitrogen dopant types and levels.308Fig. 23a–f shows the fabrication procedures for CN-CNTs and their “N” type transformation via steam etching. It was found that H2O molecules prefer to bind to carbon atoms around graphitic and pyridinic “N”, leading to selective etching. After the steam etching, therefore, pyrrolicN became the majority nitrogen form (increased from 22.1 to 55.9% among the total N species) in the nitrogen-doped carbon network (designed as: CN-H-CNT) to act as the CACs for preferential CO2RR over HER to efficiently produce CO. As shown in Fig. 23b, the steam-etched CN-H-CNT catalyst showed excellent CO2RR performance while suppressed the HER activity with a maximum faradaic efficiency of ∼88% for the formation of CO under −0.5 V vs. RHE (Fig. 23g and h).308
 Fig. 23  (a and b) The synthesis procedures of CN-H-CNTs and catalytic ability for CO2 (CO2RR) and H2O (HER) transformation. SEM images of (c) CNTs, (d) H-CNTs (H2O steam etched CNTs), (e) CN-CNTs, and (f) CN-H-CNTs. Scale bars in (c–f): 500 nm. Insets corresponding TEM images, and all the scale bars are 10 nm. (g) CV curves for the above samples in CO2-saturated 0.1 M KHCO3 meduim. (h) FE of CN-H-CNTs for CO2RR at various applied potentials versus RHE. Reproduced with permission from ref. 308, copyright 2017 to Wiley-VCH. (i) Schematic diagram of the structure of NSHCF. (j) free energy diagram of CO2RR to CO on N-doped graphene and N,S co-doped graphene. (k) LSV curves in CO2-saturated and N2-saturated 0.1 M KHCO3 solution using NSHCF900 as the cathode. (l) LSV recorded in the CO2-saturated solution by utilizing various cathodes. (m) Faradaic efficiencies of CO and H2 using NSHCF900 as the cathode. (n) Faradaic efficiencies of CO by capitalizing on various cathodes at potentials ranging from −0.3 to −1.1 vs. RHE in CO2-saturated 0.1 M KHCO3 solution. Reproduced with permission from ref. 320, copyright 2018 to Wiley-VCH. 
Furthermore, a facile and scalable route for producing N,S-codoped hierarchically porous carbon nanofiber (NSHCF, Fig. 23i) membranes (ca. 400 cm2 membrane in a single process) has been reported recently.320 DFT calculations attributed the observed outstanding CO faradaic efficiency to the presence of codoped pyridinic N and carbon-bonded S atoms, which effectively decreased the Gibbs free energy of the key *COOH intermediate (Fig. 23j). The flexible free-standing NSHCF membranes were, which could be used directly as cathodes, efficient for electrochemical CO2RR with a 94% faradaic efficiency and ∼103 mA cm−2 current density for CO production at a catalyst load of only about 1.2 mg (Fig. 23k and l).320 As can be seen in Fig. 23m and n, the faradaic efficiency for CO production increased with increasing potential from −0.3 to −0.7 V and reached its maximum value of 94% at −0.7 V vs. RHE, along with a significantly dropped H2 faradaic efficiency from 67% at −0.3 V vs. RHE to 6% at −0.7 V vs. RHE.320 Furthermore, hierarchically porous structures of the NSHCF membranes maximized the exposure of active sites for CO2RR, leading to the ultra-high current density.310 Coupled with appropriate heteroatom(s) doping and advanced morphological characteristics, therefore, the pyridinic-N dopant could promote an efficient CO2RR to produce CO with a high faradaic efficiency. The pyridinic N with a lone pair in its σ orbital could act as a Lewis base site to enhance the CO2 adsorption.66,67,287 However, the graphitic N is not favorable for CO2 binding because its electrons only fill the π* antibonding orbital.287
To demonstrate the feasibility of replacing precious metal catalysts without sacrificing the CO2RR activity or selectivity, a pyrolyzed carbon nitride and MWCNT composite were fabricated to exhibit a high selectivity for production of CO over H2 (approximately 98% CO and 2% H2) with a high activity even in an electrochemical flow cell.329 Furthermore, the CO partial current density at intermediate cathode potentials (V = −1.46 V vs. Ag/AgCl) is up to 3.5 times higher than that of the state-of-the-art Ag nano-particle-based catalysts with the maximum current density as high as 90 mA cm−2. This carbon-based catalyst has a mass activity and energy efficiency (up to 48%) higher than those of the Ag nanoparticle counterpart. Similar results have also been reported for a N-CNF catalyst, which demonstrated approximately 98% CO production efficiency and a maximum current density (∼3.5 mA cm−2) 13 times higher than that of the bulk Ag catalyst towards CO2RR, demonstrating a promising possibility for C-MFECs to replace costly metal-based CO2RR electrocatalysts.16
Apart from C-MFECs based on CNTs and graphene for CO2RR, electrochemical CO2RR studies on N-doped nanocrystalline diamonds (NDC) revealed that the incorporation of N atoms enhanced catalytic activity of both NDC-1 (diamond-like) and NDC-4 (graphitic) in reducing CO2 to CO.316 As expected, the catalytic activity enhancement by N incorporation was much more dramatic in sp2-carbon (graphitic) structures when compared to the grain boundary mediated sp3-carbon (diamond-like) structures. In NDC-1, the FE for CO generation is limited to ∼10% with HER dominating over CO generation, whereas NDC-4 shows a FE up to 82% with an excellent activity and selectivity for CO2RR to CO.316 More interestingly, nanodiamond (sp3-C hybridized) based electrode fabricated by N-doping nanodiamond/Si rod array (NDD/Si RA) was demonstrated to be efficient for CO2RR to acetate331 to preferentially convert CO2 into acetate over formic acid with an onset potential of −0.36 V and a faradaic efficiency of 91.2–91.8% at −0.8 to −1.0 V, effectively overcoming the usual low-selectivity limitation for C2 production. Electro-kinetic data and in situ infrared spectra revealed the main pathway for CO2RR to acetate: CO2 → CO2˙− → (COO)2˙ → CH3COO−.
In view of the high energy value of formic acid, considerable efforts have been made to develop various C-MFECs for CO2RR to formic acid. For instance, B-doped diamond was utilized for electroreduction of CO2 to formic acid with a faradaic efficiency as high as 95% in an aqueous solution of RbBr,332 It was demonstrated that large cations (e.g., K+, Rb+, and Cs+) could give a high faradaic efficiency for the formic acid production whereas small cations (Li+ and Na+) promoted HER instead of CO2RR. This is because of the buffer effect by large cations to suppress the increase in pH near the cathode and to maintain the high concentration of CO2 molecules near the cathode. In another study, the same research group performed the CO2RR to HCOOH in a flow cell with boron-doped diamond, demonstrating a faradaic efficiency as high as 94.7% and a selectivity as high as 99% for the production of formic acid with a stable operation for 24 hours.333 At a current density of 15 mA cm−2, the rate of production increased to 473 μmol m−2 s−1 with a faradaic efficiency of 61%. These values of the faradaic efficiency and the production rate are comparable or even superior to those achieved by Sn and Pb electrodes.333
In addition to nanodiamond-based C-MFECs for CO2RR to formic acid and formates, other examples include, but not limited to, B-doped graphene,334 phenyl functionalized C3N4,335 N-doped nanoporous carbon and CNT composites,336 N-doped graphene,337 and N-doped PC61BM ([6,6]-phenyl-C61-butyric acid methyl ester, N-C61), which exhibited an excellent activity toward CO2RR for formate production with a high FE of 91.2% and good stability at a moderate overpotential of 0.7 V.338 A partial formate current density (jHCOO−) of 14.7 mA cm−2 was observed at −1.1 V vs. RHE, which is higher than that of several C-MFECs.338Fig. 24b and c shows the synthesis procedure for N-C61 and its performance for CO2RR to formate.338 As shown in Fig. 24d, a theoretical study has suggested that graphitic N-defects were most likely the CACs for formate formation.338 Nevertheless, it has also claimed that the pyrrolic N sites on graphene were CACs for CO2 conversion to formic acid owing to its lower overpotential (0.24 V) than that of other N-doping configurations on graphene.339
 Fig. 24  (a) Schematic diagram of synthesis procedures for the N–C61 electrocatalysts. (b) CV curves of N–C61-800 in N2-saturated and CO2-saturated 0.5 M KHCO3 electrolyte. (c) Comparison of the maximum FE for formate production and the corresponding overpotentials of N–C61-800 with those of other electrocatalysts. (d) The proposed mechanism of N–C61 electrocatalysts for the CO2RR in 0.5 M KHCO3 medium. Reproduced with permission from ref. 338, copyright 2018 to Royal Society of Chemistry. (e) Faradaic efficiency of HCOOH by the CO2RR with a B-diamond electrode at 2 mA cm−2 and a flow rate of 200 mL min−1. Reproduced with permission from ref. 333, copyright 2018 to Wiley-VCH. (f) SEM image of B/N-codoped nanodiamond with B2H6 content of 12.5% and 10% N (BND3), (g) FEs for CO2 reduction on NDD, BDD, BND1 (with 2.5% N), BND2 (with 5% N), and BND3 (with 10% N) at −1.0 V vs. RHE. Reproduced with permission from ref. 340, copyright 2017 to Wiley-VCH. 
While B-doped diamond exhibited a high efficiency for CO2RR to formic acid production (Fig. 24e) as mentioned earlier,333 the B/N-codoped nanodiamond (BND) acted as an efficient and stable CO2RR electrode for C2 production of ethanol (CH3CH2OH) from CO2,340 which remained very challenging for the existing electrocatalysts due to their low activity, poor product selectivity and stability. For the BND catalyst, a good ethanol selectivity was achieved with a high faradaic efficiency of 93.2% (−1.0 V vs. RHE), which overcame the limitation of a low selectivity for multicarbon value fuels.340Fig. 24f shows the morphology of a representative BND surface with abundant grain boundaries, while Fig. 24g displays the faradaic efficiency of ethanol production for BND3 with 10% N in comparison with other catalysts.340 DFT calculations revealed the possible pathway for CO2RR towards ethanol: CO2 → *COOH → *CO → *COCO → *COCH2OH → *CH2OCH2OH → CH3CH2OH, in which the *COCO formation occurred easily on B dopants. Combined experimental analyses and DFT calculations indicated that the superior activity and selectivity of BND for CH3CH2OH production are mainly originated from the synergetic effect of B and N co-doping, the high content (up to 10%) of specific N–sp3C dopants, and the strong suppression of the competitive HER.340
Recently, various heteroatom(s) doped C-MFECs have also been reported for efficient photocatalytic CO2RR in addition to electrocatalytic CO2RR. In particular, g-C3N4 hybridized with different heteroatom-doped graphene materials were theoretically investigated for their catalytic CO2RR toward the production of deeply reduced hydrocarbons.335,341–349 It was revealed that the eight-electron reduction processes to CH4 are thermodynamically preferred on C3N4/N–G with a relatively low overall free energy change.341 Furthermore, phenyl grafted g-C3N4 was investigated for methane and formic acid production through CO2RR with a high yield,335 along with g-C3N4 for CO2 conversion to phenol,342 azine-based covalent organic frameworks (COF) as photocatalysts for CO2RR with H2O to CH4 and also for CO2 cyclic-addition to form cyclic carbonates,343,344 N-doped mesoporous carbon for ethanol production,345 N-doped carbon layer for artificial photosynthesis of CO,336 N-doped GO for ethanol and acetone production,347 and S/N-codoped nanoporous carbon electrode for efficient CO and CH4 production.348 In addition to pyridinic N, the thiophenic S was found to show a high catalytic activity and FE for CO formation.348 Moreover, N-doped graphene quantum dots (NGQDs) were demonstrated to be effective for converting CO2 into multi-carbon products at high FEs, high current densities, and low overpotentials.349 The NGQDs showed a high total FE (up to 90%) for CO2RR with a selectivity for ethylene and ethanol conversions reaching 45%. The C2 and C3 product distribution and the production rate for NGQD-catalyzed CO2RR were comparable to those obtained with copper nanoparticle-based electrocatalysts.349
Owing to their high specific surface area, chemical stability, nanoscale porosity, and structural diversity, various porous organic polymers (POPs) have been reported for selective CO2 adsorption towards efficient CO2 fixation and conversion.350 CO2 fixation and conversion on conducting polymers can be tracked back in 1992.351 POPs synthesized from large varieties of reactive monomers via simple and convenient chemical routes could be ideal adsorbents for CO2 storage and fixation. A wide range of POPs have been synthesized from different multidentate amines, aldehydes, carboxylic acids, or triazine monomers through polycondensation reactions or solid-state condensation reactions, as shown in Fig. 25.350 Ease of synthesis, uniform pore width and surface basic sites (nitrogen and other heteroelements) play a crucial role in the CO2 absorption and conversion reactions. Therefore, the design and synthesis of porous polymer networks bearing a high concentration of heteroelements could be very useful not only for enhancing the CO2 uptake, but can eventually help to promote the CO2RR for the synthesis of value-added organic fine chemicals and fuels in an efficient way. This could lead to the solution for problems of low reactivity and efficiencies associated with heterogeneous catalysis for CO2 conversion.
 Fig. 25  CO2 fixation reactions for the fuels and fine chemicals where POPs can be utilized as catalyst/catalyst supports. Adapted with permission from ref. 350, copyright 2019 to Wiley-VCH. 
CO2RR has attracted considerable research attention for producing value-added fuels and commodity chemicals. Recently, a general sol-pyrolysis process was developed to directly produce N-doped carbon nanomesh sheets from captured CO2 in ethanolamine solution.352 Construction of the C–S bond using CO2 as a C1 feedstock was also demonstrated as a green route for the synthesis of S-containing compounds and is of great significance.353 Apart from heterogeneous electrocatalysts discussed above, metal-free homogeneous electrocatalysis has also been adapted for efficient CO2RR to produce CO,354 methylamines,355 formate,356 methanol,357 methane,358 and methoxyborane.359Fig. 26 summarizes different proposed pathways toward various CO2RR products.302 Further advances in the mechanistic understanding of CO2RR are essential in order to move away from the current trial-and-error paradigm to achieve the accelerated and rational design of C-MFECs.
 Fig. 26  Each arrow stands for a proton-coupled electron transfer step except where otherwise indicated. The label +*H: surface hydrogenation. The CO pathway: yellow arrows; the formate pathway: green arrows; the ethylene pathway: red arrows; the ethanol pathway: blue arrows, and the key branching intermediates: blue and red. Reproduced with permission from ref. 302, copyright 2019 to Wiley VCH. 
CO2 evolution reaction (CO2ER) through carbon monoxide (CO) oxidation by C-MFECs is also essential to producing the raw material (CO2) required for CO2RR electrocatalysis. Also, such CO oxidation to generate CO2 is well known as a water gas shift reaction.360 Recently, N-doped penta-graphene has been proposed to be an ideal C-MFEC for CO2ER through CO electrochemical oxidation.361 Furthermore, the exploitation of CO2RR and CO2ER in energy storage systems using C-MFECs represents an alternative approach towards clean recycling and utilization of CO2.362–364 In this regard, rechargeable metal-CO2 batteries (with Li, Na, K or Mg anodes) using different C-MFEC cathodes are very promising and being extensively explored recently (vide infra).362–370


3.4.3. CMFECs for H2O2 production reaction (H2O2PR). 
Renewable hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) production represents one of the iconic examples of electrochemical transformations. This simple inorganic molecule embraces a vast number of diversified applications, including as a green oxidizer for cleaning, bleaching products for cosmetic (e.g., hair) as well as the paper and pulp industries, fuel to power a rocket, and a clean energy carrier to run automobiles, making H2O2 a very versatile and multifunctional chemical related to a market of almost 4 billion dollars and projected to reach 6 billion by 2023.371–375 However, the industrial production of H2O2 still relies on a very energy-intensive synthetic process called the anthraquinone process that involves a multistep sequence consisting of a hydrogenation step over Pd catalyst, followed by oxidation and extraction, generating organic wastes from inefficient oxidation of the anthraquinone.373,376 In view of the importance of H2O2 mentioned above, there is a recent surge of interest in developing simple and sustainable protocols for cost-effective electrocatalytic/photocatalytic syntheses of H2O2 at ambient conditions.14,377–419 Nevertheless, catalysts based on precious noble metals (Pd, Pt, etc.), transition metals (Ag, etc.), metal oxides (e.g., BiVO4), bimetallic alloys (Pd–Hg) are required for the traditional electrocatalytic synthesis of H2O2 from ORR with a low yield, but a high energy consumption and H2 supply.382,384 In this context, C-MFECs are very promising as efficient and stable electrocatalytic/photocatalytic electrode materials for H2O2PR as ORR via a two-electron pathway can lead to efficient electro-/photo-synthesis of H2O2.380–382,384
Recently, numerous C-MFECs have been reported for efficient electrocatalytic and photocatalytic H2O2PR. Examples include, but not limited to, C-dots,385 hierarchical porous carbons,386 defective carbon materials,387 fully or partially reduced graphene oxide-based C-MFECs,382,388–390 O-doped CNTs,7 edge-rich O-doped nanocarbons,391 lattice defect-rich O-doped graphene,392 activated carbon black cathode recycled from a zinc-air battery (ZAB),393 redox-active cationic covalent triazine networks,394 PEDOTs,395 activated porous carbon fibers,396 N-doped porous carbon derived from biomass,397 N-doped mesoporous carbon,398 N-doped graphitic C-nanohorns,399 ionic-liquid derived N-doped porous carbons,400 hard-template (SBA-15 silica) assisted N-doped orderly porous carbons,401 other N-doped porous carbons,14,402 N-doped graphene,403 F-doped porous carbons,404 C and N vacancy abundant g-C3N4,405,406 halogen-doped C3N4,407 holey C3N4,408 phosphate modified g-C3N4,409 defect containing C3N4,410,411 BN quantum dot and g-C3N4 hybrids,412 pyromellitic diimide (PDI) doped g-C3N4 and rGO hybrids,413 PDI-doped g-C3N4/BN/rGO hybrids,414 black P and polymeric-C3N4 hybrids,415 P-doped CNTs,416 N/S-codoped porous carbons,417,418 and N/P/Otridoped carbons derived from biomass.419 Among various N-doped defective C-MFECs, N-doped graphene derived from oxo-G with the lowest in-plane carbon lattice defects exhibited the highest H2O2 selectivity of >82% in 0.1 M KOH solution.392
Of particular interest, a highly efficient C-MFEC with abundant active and oxygenated graphitic edge sites (Fig. 27a and b) was prepared through the infiltration of an aromatic carbon precursor into mesoporous silica of a hexagonal mesostructure, followed by graphitization and then removal of the silica template.391 The mesopores in the silica template acted as nanoreactors, within which confined graphitization generated nano-sized graphite, while the silica framework, upon the removal, was transformed into mesopores. The resultant edge-rich orderly mesoporous carbon (GOMC) catalyst exhibited ca. 28 times higher activity and a H2O2 selectivity over 90% for H2O2PR than the basal plane-rich carbon nanotubes. The optimized nanocarbon catalyst showed a very high H2O2PR activity, surpassing previously-reported catalysts in alkaline media.391 Moreover, it can stably produce H2O2 for 16 h with a FE reaching 99% (Fig. 27c) and an accumulated H2O2 concentration of 24 ± 2 mM due to a fast electron transfer for the oxidized and edge-rich carbon nanocatalysts.391 This work provides an important guideline for the design and development of highly efficient carbon-based electrocatalysts for H2O2PR.
 Fig. 27  (a) The side-view TEM image of the edge-site rich graphitic ordered mesoporous carbon (GOMC) (inset: top-view, scale bar: 25 nm), (b) HR-TEM of GOMC, (c) chronoamperometic response of o-GOMC-1 for H2O2PR in O2-saturated 0.1 M KOH solution in an H-type cell. Reproduced with permission from ref. 391, copyright 2019 to Wiley-VCH. (d) Structural details of N-doped graphitic C-nanohorn (CNH) along with mechanism for H2O2 production, (e) plots of FE percentage versus time, (f) a stability test at pH 1.0, 7.4, and 13.0 over 24 h was carried out with a three-electrode setup comprising a working electrode made of g-N-CNH modified glassy carbon, a counter electrode made of Pt net separated from the bulk solution by a porous membrane, and a reference Ag/AgCl electrode. Reproduced with permission from ref. 399, copyright 2018 to Elsevier Inc. (g) Illustration of liquid Zn–air battery based on the a CB electrocatalyst with the cogeneration of electricity and H2O2. (h) Polarization curve and power density plot of the Zn–air battery. (i) Potentiostatic discharge of the Zn–air battery at a given potential of 0.8 V. Reproduced with permission from ref. 393, copyright 2018 to American Chemical Society. 
In another study, graphitized N-doped single-walled carbon nanohorns (CNHs) were fabricated for electrocatalytic H2O2PR with a high selectivity and FE.399Fig. 27d schematically shows the CNH structure and the associated H2O2PR mechanism.399 This catalyst was very efficient in acidic pH with a FE of about 98% and also showed excellent H2O2PR activities at either physiological or alkaline pH (Fig. 27e and f). Moreover, the very positive onset potentials observed at three different pH values (+0.40, +0.53, and +0.71 V at pH 1.0, 7.4, and 13.0, respectively), along with its good stability and excellent reproducibility, made this CNH material a benchmark catalyst for metal-free ORR to produce H2O2.399
Another attractive option to produce H2O2 is artificial photosynthesis by converting solar energy into chemical energy.414 For this purpose, g-C3N4 was functionalized by PDI and then hybridized with both BN and rGO nanosheets to fabricate g-C3N4–PDI/BN/rGO nanocomposites to produce H2O2 from water and O2 in the ambient atmosphere under visible light.414 In this case, BN and rGO acted as the trapping sites for the holes and electrons, respectively, on the photoexcited g-C3N4/PDI moiety to enhance the water oxidation and selective two-electron reduction of O2. This resulted in an efficient H2O2 production with a solar-to-chemical conversion efficiency of 0.27%, which was higher than that achieved by overall water splitting with powdered catalysts.414
To demonstrated the possible use of renewable energy as the power source for electrocatalytic production of H2O2, a self-powered system based on a Zn–air battery using the oxygenated activated carbon black (aCB) C-MFEC cathode was constructed to simultaneously produce electricity and H2O2, as shown in Fig. 27g–i.393 The battery with a power density of 360 W mgeo−2 at an operating voltage of 0.8 V exhibited a high H2O2 production rate of 5.93 mol mgeo−2 h−1. DFT calculations revealed that C–O–C and –CHO functional groups in aCB contributed more to the H2O2 production compared to other functional groups.393 The development of effective and energy-efficient water disinfection technologies coupled with H2O2 production systems is extremely urgent to relieve clean water scarcity and global energy shortage, more fundamental studies, along with detailed mechanistic understanding, are required to further develop H2O2PR-assisted water disinfection based on C-MFECs.

4. Multifunctional C-MFECs for energy devices and integrated energy systems
Imparting multifunctionality in C-MFECs for cost-effective construction of energy devices and/or integrated energy systems with a single metal-free electrocatalyst has attracted increasing attention recently. By creating a variety of co-existing active sites (e.g., doping with more than one different heteroatoms), C-MFECs can possess multiple catalytic functionalities, which is otherwise difficult using metal-based catalysts. This significant advantage allows for the design of new C-MFECs capable of catalyzing different electrochemical reactions simultaneously, such as the ORR in fuel cells (Fig. 28a),420 ORR and OER for metal–air batteries (MABs, Fig. 28b),421 OER and HER for overall water splitting (Fig. 28c),422 and CO2 reduction and evolution for metal-CO2 batteries (Fig. 28d).262
 Fig. 28  (a) The configuration and mechanism of operation of a hydrogen fuel cell, adapted from ref. 420. (b) Detailed electrode configuration for a metal-air battery, reproduced with permission from ref. 421 (copyright 2016 to Royal Society of Chemistry). (c) Water splitting cell assisted by solar light, reproduced from ref. 422 (copyright 2012 to American Chemical Society). (d) Schematic representation of a CO2 emission and conversion cycle, adapted with permission from ref. 262 (copyright 2019 to American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS)). 
Trifunctional C-MFECs efficient for ORR, OER, and HER can be utilized for MAB and water splitting integrated systems.8,38,131 In this regard, S/N-codoped graphitic carbon sheets containing stereoscopic holes and defect-rich N-doped ultrathin carbon nanosheets have been developed to show excellent ORR–OER–HER trifunctional electrocatalysis.38,131 Moreover, multifunctional electrocatalysis was also achieved over a wide pH range on C60/SWCNT hybrid electrode.46 Continued research and more in-depth mechanistic understanding along this direction should lead to the development of more advanced multifunctional C-MFECs for various electrocatalytic reactions, such as ORR, OER, HER, NRR, CO2RR, and H2O2PR, in many more integrated devices/systems, as those to be discussed below.
4.1. Recent advances in C-MFECs for ORR in fuel cells
Fuel cells oxidize a fuel via electrochemical catalytic reactions without burning,4,423 thereby avoiding the inefficiencies and pollution associated with traditional combustion technologies. Thus, fuel cells produce clean electricity with no pollutant emission. As shown in Fig. 28a, hydrogen oxidation occurs (from H2 to H+ ions) at the anode in a hydrogen fuel cell to generate electricity while the electrocatalytic ORR (O2 to OH− ions or H2O) takes place on the cathode.420 As listed in Table 4, however, there are a large variety of different type fuel cells with their most effective operation temperature being determined by the nature of electrolyte and charge-carrying medium, from about 60 °C for the polymer electrolyte membrane fuel cell (PEMFC) up to 1000 °C for the solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC).424 Owing to their chemical stability, high active surface area, good conducting property, and excellent thermomechanical reliability, C-MFECs are generally promised to replace Pt-based cathodes in PEMFCs, phosphoric acid fuel cells (PAFCs), and alkaline fuel cells (AFCs) operating at 60–80 °C, ∼200 °C, and 60–220 °C, respectively.8,425,426 Carbon electrodes may be oxidized and become unstable at a high temperature. Therefore, molten carbonate fuel cells (MCFCs, 650 °C) and solid-oxide fuel cells (SOFCs, 600–1000 °C) work mainly with metal-based and ceramic electrodes, respectively.425
